حرام فما ظنك في الصلاة ا هـ . وأراد به كراهة التحريم وأورد عليه في غاية البيان بأنه إذا كان حراماً ينبغي أن يكون مفسداً كالقهقهة، المية . وحمل فعله على أنه بيان للجواز مبني على ما قاله وإلا فدعوى الجواز في المكروه تحريماً ممنوعة . قلت : وينبغي التوفيق بحمل القول الأول على ما إذا دعت إلى مسحه حاجة ويكون تركه حينئذ أولى على نحو ما يأتي في قلت الحصى، وحمل الثاني على ما إذا لم تدع إليه حاجة فليتأمل . قوله : (بعد الفراغ من الصلاة) لأن فيه إزالة الأذى عن نفسه فلا بأس به بل يستحب كما في الذخيرة، وإنما كره إذا كان في وسط الصلاة وكان لا يضره لأنه لا يفيد لأنه يسجد بعده بخلاف
His statement: 'And he disliked his fidgeting with his garment and his body, and turning over pebbles except for one prostration, and cracking the fingers, and placing the hands on the waist, and turning away' is the beginning of explaining the disliked actions after explaining the invalidators, because each of them is from the accidents, but he gave precedence to the invalidator due to its strength. And the disliked in this chapter are two types: one of them is that which is disliked with tahrimi severity, and that is the interpretation when they unconditionally mention 'disliked' as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir from the Book of Zakat, and he mentioned that it is in the rank of the obligatory, not established except by what establishes the obligatory, meaning by a prohibition that is zanni in its thubut, for the obligatory is established by a command that is zanni in its thubut. The second is the tanzihi disliked, and its reference is that leaving it is better, and they often use it as mentioned by al-Allama al-Halabi in the issue of wiping sweat. So when they mention a disliked action, one must consider its evidence. If it is a zanni prohibition, it is judged as makruh tahrimi unless there is a diverting factor that turns the prohibition from tahrim to nadb. But if the evidence is not a prohibition but rather indicates non-absolute abandonment, then it is tanzihi. And they differed in the interpretation of 'abath. Al-Kardari mentioned that it is an action in which there is a non-shar'i purpose, and safah (foolishness) is that in which there is no purpose at all. And what is mentioned in Sharh al-Hidayah and other works is that 'abath is an action for an incorrect purpose, until it is said in al-Nihayah: its summary is that every action that is beneficial for the worshipper, then there is no harm in him doing it. Its origin is that it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) sweated in prayer and wiped the sweat from his forehead, i.e., he wiped it, because it was harming him, so it was beneficial. And in the summer, when he rose from prostration, he would shake his garment to the right or left, because it was beneficial so that no trace remains. As for that which is not beneficial, it is 'abath. End. Al-Allama al-Halabi criticized him by saying that if it is disliked to lift the garment so that it does not gather dust, and that there is disagreement on whether it is disliked to wipe dust from one's forehead in prayer, and that it is recommended to make the face dusty in prostration, let alone the garment, then the claim that shaking the garment from dust is a beneficial action and that there is no harm in it absolutely is seemingly questionable. As for that there is no harm in wiping sweat in prayer, that is the saying of some mashayikh, and it was chosen in al-Khaniyah and other works. And in Munyat al-Musalli: It is disliked to wipe one's sweat or dust from his forehead during the prayer or in the tashahhud before the salam. And reconciliation was made between them by the meaning of the wiped sweat is sweat for which there is no need to wipe it, and the disliked is tanzihi disliked. So then there is no contradiction between it and their saying 'there is no harm' because leaving it is better, and his action if established is to be interpreted that he had a need to wipe it, or as an illustration of permissibility. End. And in al-Khaniyah: There is no harm in wiping one's forehead from dust or grass after finishing the prayer, and before it if that harms him and distracts him from prayer. But if it does not harm him, it is disliked in the middle of the prayer, and not disliked before the tashahhud and salam. End. And he authenticated it in al-Muhit. And along with what we have presented of the definition of 'abath, it indicates that scratching one's body with his hand is only 'abath if it is without need. But if something bites him in his body, harms him, and distracts him, then there is no harm in scratching it, and it is not considered 'abath. Then the commentators mentioned that they only introduced the issue of 'abath because it is general and others are specific because turning over pebbles, cracking fingers, and placing hands on waist are types of 'abath, and the general is presented before the specific. And it was criticized in al-Inayah that 'abath with the garment does not include what follows like turning over pebbles and others; rather they introduced it because it occurs more frequently! End. And it may be said that what encompasses turning over and others is 'abath with the body, and he did not say that unless they limited themselves to 'abath with the garment. Then, the dislike of 'abath is tahrimi according to what al-Quda'i related in Musnad al-Shihab as a mursal from Yahya ibn Abi Kathir from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Verily Allah has disliked for you three things: fidgeting in prayer, obscenity in fasting, and laughing in graveyards.' And he gave the reason in al-Hidayah that fidgeting outside prayer is haram, so what do you think about in prayer? End. He intended by it makruh tahrimi. And it was objected to in Ghayat al-Bayan that if it is haram, it should be invalidating like loud laughter. 'al-miyah'? And interpreting his action as an illustration of permissibility is based on what he said, otherwise the claim of permissibility in that which is makruh tahrimi is prohibited. I say: Reconciliation should be made by interpreting the first saying as when there is a need to wipe, and in that case leaving it is better similar to what will come in 'you may turn over pebbles', and interpreting the second as when there is no need to wipe, so reflect. His statement: 'after finishing the prayer' because it involves removing harm from himself, so there is no harm, rather it is recommended as in al-Dhakhirah. It is only disliked if it is in the middle of the prayer and does not harm him because it is not beneficial since he prostrates afterwards, unlike ...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer, specifically focusing on 'abath (idle fidgeting). The author begins by distinguishing between two types of makruh: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked). In the Hanafi school, when the term 'makruh' is used without qualification, it generally means makruh tahrimi, which is established by a zanni (speculative) prohibition. The second type, makruh tanzihi, is where leaving the action is better but it is not prohibited. The text then defines 'abath: according to al-Kardari, it is an action with a non-shar'i purpose; according to Sharh al-Hidayah, it is an action for an incorrect purpose, and the criterion is benefit—any action that benefits the worshipper is not 'abath. The proof is the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) wiping sweat from his forehead during prayer because it was harming him, and shaking his garment in summer to remove dust. This shows that actions necessitated by need are not 'abath. The commentator then discusses a disagreement: some scholars (like in al-Khaniyah) permit wiping sweat or dust if there is a genuine need, while others (in Munyat al-Musalli) consider it makruh tanzihi. A reconciliation is offered by differentiating between need and habit. The passage also states that the dislike of 'abath is tahrimi, citing a hadith from al-Quda'i. However, an objection is raised: if 'abath were haram, it would invalidate prayer like loud laughter. The commentator suggests that the first opinion (permissibility) applies when there is a need, and in such cases leaving it is still better; the second opinion (dislike) applies when there is no need. Finally, the text notes that wiping dust after prayer is recommended, but during prayer it is disliked unless it distracts the worshipper. This lesson clarifies that not all actions performed during prayer are automatically 'abath; the underlying principle is utility and necessity.

Key Terms
العبث (al-'abath): Idle fidgeting or any action in prayer without a valid benefit or necessity. Defined as an action for a non-shar'i or incorrect purpose.
المكروه تحريماً (al-makruh tahriman): An action that is prohibitively disliked, established by a zanni (speculative) prohibition. It is in the level of wajib in terms of proof strength but is not obligatory to avoid.
المكروه تنزيهاً (al-makruh tanzihan): An action that is slightly disliked; leaving it is better but it is not prohibited. The evidence does not constitute a prohibition but indicates that avoidance is preferable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the ruling on 'abath considered makruh tahrimi rather than haram according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the hadith is mursal and thus zanni al-thubut, so it does not reach the level of qat'i prohibition.
B) Because 'abath does not invalidate prayer.
C) Because the Prophet disliked it but it is not prohibited by any evidence.
D) Because it is only disliked in prayer, not outside.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'abath (idle fidgeting) in prayer is makruh tahrimi, but actions necessitated by genuine need are exempt and considered permissible or at most makruh tanzihi.

Definition and Ruling of al-ʿAbath (Fidgeting) in Prayer
Defining العبث and discussing its ruling and applications such as wiping sweat/dust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ʿabath according to the Hanafi school, state its ruling (makruh tahrimi), and apply the distinction between beneficial and non-beneficial actions to cases like wiping sweat or dust during prayer.

Core Idea
In prayer, every non-beneficial action is ʿabath and makruh tahrimi; beneficial actions are permissible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on unnecessary movements (al-ʿabath) during prayer. Using the definition from al-Nihayah, we distinguish between actions that benefit the worshipper (like wiping harmful sweat) and those that are futile. The discussion includes the proof from a mursal hadith and addresses objections regarding invalidation.

Guided Path
1
Define al-ʿabath
Understand that ʿabath is any action in prayer that is not beneficial (ghayr mufid) to the worshipper. The source text defines it as action for an invalid purpose.
Benefit is judged by whether the action removes harm or aids concentration.
2
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: ʿabath is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). This is based on the mursal hadith from al-Qudaʿi and the analogy that ʿabath outside prayer is haram, so it is even more so inside prayer.
The ruling does not invalidate prayer unless the action becomes excessive (much action).
3
Apply the beneficial/non-beneficial distinction
Analyze examples such as wiping sweat, shaking dust off clothes, and scratching. If the action is beneficial (e.g., removes harm, aids focus), it is permissible. If non-beneficial, it is ʿabath and makruh tahrimi.
Context matters: if sweat harms and distracts, wiping is beneficial; otherwise, it is disliked (tanzih).
4
Evaluate the objection regarding invalidation
Some argue that if ʿabath is haram, it should invalidate prayer like qahqaha (laughter). The Hanafi response: qahqaha invalidates because it breaks wudu, not because it is haram. Minor ʿabath without breaking wudu does not invalidate.
Excessive ʿabath (much action) does invalidate prayer, similar to other haram acts that are not condoned.

Source Text

والمذكور في شرح الهداية وغيرها أن العبث الفعل لغرض غير صحيح حتى قال في النهاية : وحاصله أن كل عمل هو مفيد للمصلي فلا بأس بأن يأتي به أصله ما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عرق في صلاة فسلت العرق عن جبينه أي مسحه لأنه كان يؤذيه فكان مفيداً . وفي زمن الصيف كان إذا قام من السجود نفض ثوبه يمنة أو يسرة لأنه كان مفيداً كيلا يبقى صورة، فأما ما ليس بمفيد فهو العبث اهـ. وتعقبه العلامة الحلبي بأنه إذا كان يكره رفع الثوب كيلا يتترب وأنه قد وقع الخلاف في أنه يكره مسح التراب عن جبهته في الصلاة وأنه قد وقع الندب إلى تتريب الوجه في السجود فضلاً عن الثوب فكون نقض الثوب عن التراب عملاً مفيداً وأنه لا بأس به السلام، ووفق بينهما بأن المراد بالعرق الممسوح عرق لم تدعه حاجة إلى مسحه وبالكراهة الكراهة التنزيهية فحينئذ لا منافاة بينها وبين قولهم لا بأس لأن تركه أولى ويحمل فعله إن ثبت على أن به حاجة إلى مسحه أو بياناً للجواز ا.هـ. وفي الخانية : ولا بأس بأن يمسح جبهته من التراب أو الحشيش بعد الفراغ من الصلاة وقبله إذا كان يضره ذلك ويشغله عن الصلاة، وإذا كان لا يضره ذلك يكره في وسط الصلاة ولا يكره قبل التشهد والسلام ا هـ. وصححه في المحيط وهو مع ما قدمناه من تعريف العبث يدل على أن الحك بيده في بدنه إنما يكون عبئاً إذا كان لغير حاجة، أما إذا أكله شيء في بدنه ضره وأشغله فلا بأس بحكه ولا يكون من العبث. ثم ذكر الشارحون أنهم إنما قدموا مسألة العبث لأنها كلية وغيرها نوعية لأن تقليب الحصى والفرقعة والتخصر من أنواع العبث والكلي مقدم على النوعي وتعقبه في العناية بأن العبث بالثوب لا يشمل ما بعده من تقليب الحصى وغيره بل إنما قدموه لأنه أكثر وقوعاً !. اهـ. وقد يقال : إن الشامل للتقليب وغيره العبث بالبدن ولا . ما قاله إلا لو اقتصروا على العبث بالثوب . ثم إن كراهة العبث تحريمية لما أخرجه القضاعي في مسند الشهاب مرسلاً عن يحيى بن أبي كثير عن النبي ( إن الله كره لكم ثلاثاً العبث في الصلاة والرفث في الصيام والضحك في المقابر» وعلله في الهداية بأن العبث خارج الصلاة حرام فما ظنك في الصلاة ا هـ . وأراد به كراهة التحريم وأورد عليه في غاية البيان بأنه إذا كان حراماً ينبغي أن يكون مفسداً كالقهقهة، المية . وحمل فعله على أنه بيان للجواز مبني على ما قاله وإلا فدعوى الجواز في المكروه تحريماً ممنوعة . قلت : وينبغي التوفيق بحمل القول الأول على ما إذا دعت إلى مسحه حاجة ويكون تركه حينئذ أولى على نحو ما يأتي في قلت الحصى، وحمل الثاني على ما إذا لم تدع إليه حاجة فليتأمل . قوله : (بعد الفراغ من الصلاة) لأن فيه إزالة الأذى عن نفسه فلا بأس به بل يستحب كما في الذخيرة، وإنما كره إذا كان في وسط الصلاة وكان لا يضره لأنه لا يفيد لأنه يسجد بعده بخلاف وأجاب بأن فساد القهقهة لا باعتبار حرمتها بل باعتبار أنها تنقض الطهارة وهي شرط ولهذا لا يفسدها النظر إلى الأجنبية وإن كان حراماً إلا إذا كثر العبث فحينئذ يفسدها لكونه عملاً كثيراً. وفي الغاية للسروجي قوله ولأن العبث خارج الصلاة حرام» فيه نظر لأن العبث خارجها بثوبه أو بدنه خلاف الأولى ولا يحرم والحديث قيد بكونه في الصلاة ا هـ.
It is mentioned in Sharh al-Hidayah and others that al-ʿabath is an action for an invalid purpose. In al-Nihayah: its summary is that every action beneficial for the worshipper is permissible; originally it is related that the Prophet ﷺ sweat during prayer and wiped the sweat from his forehead because it harmed him, and that was beneficial. In summer, when he rose from prostration, he would shake his garment right or left because it was beneficial to avoid leaving an impression. Whatever is not beneficial is al-ʿabath. Al-Halabi objected that if raising the garment to avoid dust is disliked, and there is disagreement on wiping dust from the forehead in prayer, and the face is commanded to be dusted in prostration, let alone the garment, then saying that shaking the garment from dust is beneficial and absolutely permissible is evidently questionable. As for saying that wiping sweat in prayer is permissible, it is the view of some mashayikh and is chosen in al-Khaniyyah and others. In Munyat al-Musalli: it is disliked to wipe sweat or dust from one's forehead during prayer or in tashahhud before salam. Reconciliation: the sweat meant is that for which there is no need to wipe, and the dislike is tanzih; so there is no conflict between 'no harm' and 'first to leave it', and his action is interpreted as due to need or as showing permissibility. In al-Khaniyyah: there is no harm in wiping dust or grass from his forehead after prayer and before it if it harms him and distracts him; if it does not harm him, it is disliked in the middle of prayer but not before tashahhud and salam. It is corrected in al-Muhit, and with our earlier definition of ʿabath it indicates that scratching with the hand on his body is only ʿabath if without need; if something bites him and harms and distracts him, there is no harm, and it is not ʿabath. Then the commentators said that they introduced the issue of ʿabath because it is universal and others are specific; turning pebbles, cracking fingers, and placing hands on hips are types of ʿabath, and the universal precedes the specific. Al-ʿInayah objected that ʿabath with the garment does not include what follows like turning pebbles, but they introduced it because it occurs more often. It may be said that the general term for turning and other things is ʿabath with the body. What he said is only if they limited it to ʿabath with the garment. Then the karahah of ʿabath is tahrimi because of what al-Qudaʿi transmitted in Musnad al-Shihab as a mursal from Yahya ibn Abi Kathir from the Prophet ﷺ: 'Allah has disliked for you three things: ʿabath in prayer, rafath in fasting, and laughing in graveyards.' Al-Hidayah explained it: ʿabath outside prayer is haram, so what do you think about inside prayer? He intended karahah tahrim. An objection was raised in Ghayat al-Bayan: if it is haram, it should invalidate like qahqaha. (Then an unclear word). The author then gives reconciliation. Then the text comments on 'after prayer' because it removes harm, so no harm, rather recommended, and it is disliked in the middle of prayer if no harm. He answered that invalidation by qahqaha is not due to its prohibition but because it nullifies wudu, which is a condition; hence looking at a non-mahram does not invalidate, though haram, unless the ʿabath is excessive, then it invalidates as much action. In al-Ghayah for al-Saruji: his statement 'because ʿabath outside prayer is haram' is questionable, because ʿabath outside with garment or body is not the best, but not haram, and the hadith restricts it to being in prayer.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. It begins by defining al-ʿabath (fidgeting) as any action done for an invalid purpose. The author cites al-Nihayah that the criterion is benefit: any action beneficial to the worshipper is permissible, while non-beneficial action is ʿabath. He supports this with reports that the Prophet ﷺ wiped sweat from his forehead during prayer and shook his garment after prostration, because these actions were beneficial. Then he enters a detailed discussion on wiping sweat and dust during prayer. Al-Halabi questioned the categorization of dusting a garment as always beneficial, given that raising a garment to avoid dust is disliked and the face is commanded to be dusted in prostration. The author reconciles conflicting reports: wiping sweat or dust without need is disliked with tanzih (not tahrim), but if there is genuine need (e.g., harm or distraction), it is permissible. He cites Munyat al-Musalli and al-Khaniyyah, distinguishing between middle of prayer and before salam. The correct view (mufta bihi) is that if it harms or distracts, there is no harm; otherwise, it is disliked. The author then links this to the general definition of ʿabath: any non-beneficial action in prayer is ʿabath and is makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked). This is based on a mursal hadith transmitted by al-Qudaʿi that Allah dislikes three things, including ʿabath in prayer. The author of al-Hidayah argued that since ʿabath outside prayer is haram, it is even more so in prayer. An objection was raised: if it is haram, it should invalidate prayer like laughter (qahqaha). The response is that qahqaha invalidates not because it is haram but because it breaks wudu (a condition for prayer). Therefore, minor ʿabath without breaking wudu does not invalidate but is still haram. However, the author of al-Ghayah argued that ʿabath outside prayer is not haram but merely contrary to the better choice. The text concludes that the hadith explicitly mentions ʿabath in prayer, so the ruling is specific to prayer. The overall ruling: any non-beneficial action in prayer is makruh tahriman, and beneficial actions are permissible.

Key Terms
العبث (al-ʿabath): Any action in prayer that is not beneficial (ghayr mufid) for the worshipper, i.e., done for an invalid purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wiping sweat from one's forehead during prayer if the sweat is harming and distracting the worshipper?
A) It is haram and invalidates the prayer.
B) It is permissible (la ba'sa) and not considered ʿabath.
C) It is makruh tanzih (mildly disliked) in all cases.
D) It is wajib to remove the harm.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, non-beneficial movements in prayer are prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) but do not invalidate prayer unless they become excessive, while beneficial movements are permissible.

Ruling on Flipping Pebbles in Prayer
Clarifying the ruling on smoothing pebbles for prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on flipping pebbles in prayer and identify when it is permissible and when it is disliked.

Core Idea
Smoothing pebbles once for valid prostration is a concession; more than once is disliked.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on smoothing pebbles during prayer. While unnecessary fidgeting is discouraged, the school recognizes that a worshipper may need to level the ground for a valid prostration. The sources weigh the need against the principle of khushuʿ, leading to a distinction between necessary and unnecessary movements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is flipping or smoothing pebbles during prayer. The basic ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked) without necessity.
Distinguish between fidgeting (ʿabath) and necessary preparation.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Muʿayqib: 'Do not wipe the pebbles while you are praying; if you must do it, then once.' This is found in the six major hadith collections.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted for rulings of karahah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative prohibition 'la tamsh' indicates dislike (karahah). The conditional permission 'if you must, then once' limits the permissible to one movement, implying that more than once is prohibited or disliked.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr ba‘da al-nahy yufidu al-ibahah (command after prohibition indicates permissibility).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars consider smoothing once a concession (rukhsah) and leaving it better; others consider leaving it the preferred practice (azima) because it is closer to khushuʿ. The text sides with leaving being better.
This is a difference within the school, not a fundamental dispute.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that smoothing once is permissible if needed, but leaving it is more virtuous. More than one movement is makruh.
The tarjih is based on the hadith's emphasis on leaving and the principle of avoiding innovation (bidʿah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقلب الحصى إلا للسجود (مرة أي كره قلبه لغير ضرورة لما أخرج في الكتب الستة عن معيقيب أنه صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لا تمسح الحصى وأنت تصلي فإن كنت لا بد فاعلاً فواحدة»(۱) وعن أبي ذر أنه قال : سألت خليلي عن كل شيء حتى سألته عن تسوية الحصى في الصلاة فقال : يا أبا ذر مرة أو ذر. ولأنه نوع عبث، أما إذا كان لا يمكنه السجود عليه فيسويه مرة لأن فيه إصلاح صلاته كذا في الهداية يعني فيه تحصيل السجود على الوجه المطلوب شرعاً وهو يفيد أن تسويته مرة لهذا الغرض أولى من تركها. وصرح في البدائع بأن التسوية مرة رخصة وأن الترك أولى لأنه أقرب إلى الخشوع. وفي النهاية والخلاصة : إن الترك أحب إلي مستدلاً في النهاية بما ورد عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في بعض الروايات وإن تركتها فهو خير لك من مائة ناقة سوداء الحدقة تكون لك»(۲) اهـ. فالحاصل أن التسوية لغرض صحيح مرة هل هي رخصة أو عزيمة وقد تعارض فيها جهتان، فبالنظر إلى أن التسوية مقتضية للسجود على الوجه المسنون كانت التسوية عزيمة، وبالنظر إلى أن تركها أقرب إلى الخشوع كان تركها عزيمة والظاهر من الأحاديث الثاني ويرجحه أن الحكم إذا تردد بين سنة وبدعة كان ترك البدعة راجحاً على فعل السنة مع أنه قد كان يمكنه التسوية قبل الشروع في الصلاة وتقييد المصنف بالمرة هو ظاهر الرواية والزيادة عليها مكروهة، وقيل يسويها مرتين ذكره في منية المصلي
His statement: (and flipping pebbles except for prostration) i.e., it is disliked to flip them without necessity because of what is reported in the six books from Muʿayqib that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Do not wipe the pebbles while you are praying; if you must do it, then once.' And from Abu Dharr: 'I asked my close friend about everything, even about smoothing pebbles in prayer, and he said: O Abu Dharr, once or leave.' And because it is a type of ʿabath. But if he cannot prostrate on them, he smooths them once because it rectifies his prayer, as in al-Hidayah, meaning it achieves prostration in the legally desired manner, and this indicates that smoothing them once for this purpose is better than leaving it. In al-Bada'iʿ it is stated that smoothing once is a concession (rukhsah) and leaving it is better because it is closer to khushuʿ. In al-Nihayah and al-Khulasah: leaving it is more beloved to me, supporting it with what is reported from the Prophet ﷺ in some narrations: 'If you leave it, it is better for you than one hundred black-eyed camels.' So the conclusion: is smoothing for a valid purpose once a concession or an obligation? Two aspects conflict: from the perspective that smoothing enables prostration in the sunnah manner, smoothing is an obligation; from the perspective that leaving is closer to khushuʿ, leaving is an obligation. The apparent from the hadith is the second, and it is strengthened by the principle that when a ruling is between a sunnah and an innovation, leaving the innovation is better than doing the sunnah, especially since he could have smoothed before starting prayer. The author's restriction to once is the apparent narration, and more than that is disliked. It is said he may smooth twice, mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This lesson deals with the ruling on flipping or smoothing pebbles (clean earth or gravel) during prayer to prepare for prostration. The matn states that flipping pebbles is disliked except for prostration, and only once. The author explains that flipping without need is disliked because it is a type of ʿabath, and cites the hadith of Muʿayqib that the Prophet ﷺ forbade wiping pebbles and allowed only one movement. If the worshipper cannot prostrate properly on the ground because of the pebbles, then smoothing once is a concession (rukhsah) because it corrects his prayer. However, there is a tension: some scholars (like in al-Bada'iʿ) say smoothing once is a rukhsa and leaving it is better; others (like in al-Nihayah) strongly prefer leaving it. The author resolves that the stronger view is that leaving it is better (azima) because it is closer to khushuʿ and the hadith emphasizes leaving it over many camels. The principle is that if the action is between a sunnah (smoothing for valid prostration) and an innovation (making unnecessary movements), avoiding innovation is preferable. Moreover, the worshipper could have prepared the place before prayer. The reliance is that if needed, once is permissible but more than once is disliked.

Key Terms
قلب الحصى (qalb al-hasa): Flipping or smoothing small pebbles on the ground with the hand during prayer to make a level surface for prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred action regarding smoothing pebbles in prayer if they are not hindering prostration?
A) It is permissible to smooth them once.
B) It is obligatory to smooth them once.
C) It is better to leave them and not smooth at all.
D) It is permissible to smooth them up to three times.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position distinguishes between necessary and unnecessary movements in prayer; smoothing pebbles is only a concession when needed, and leaving it is closer to khushuʿ.

Ruling on Cracking Fingers in Prayer
Ruling on cracking fingers in prayer with consensus and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cracking fingers in prayer and explain the two dalil (consensus and weak hadith) that support its dislike.

Core Idea
Cracking fingers in prayer is disliked (makruh) due to consensus and a weak hadith; it is equated with looking around.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on cracking fingers (farqaʿat al-asabiʿ) during prayer. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra’iq cites a consensus (ijmaʿ) and two prophetic narrations to establish the dislike. Even though the hadith chains are weak, the consensus strengthens the ruling. We will dissect the evidence and understand why the Hanafi school considers this action makruh tanzih (mildly disliked).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cracking fingers (pressing/stretching fingers to produce sound). The ruling is makruh tanzih (mildly disliked). The author defines the act and states there is consensus on its dislike.
This is the starting point for any fiqh analysis: know what is being ruled and the level of hukm.
2
Locate the dalil
There are two proofs: (a) consensus (ijmaʿ) as cited from al-Dirayah; (b) a hadith from Ibn Majah with a weak chain due to al-Harith, and another from Sahal ibn Muʿadh equating it with laughing and looking around. The author uses both ijmaʿ and sunnah. The ijmaʿ provides certainty, while the hadith provides textual basis.
The hadith is ahad and weak, so the ruling is not based solely on it.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition 'la tafqaʿ' indicates nahy (prohibition). In Hanafi usul, a nahy from a weak hadith gives karahah tanzih, not tahrim. The second hadith equates it with looking around (which is makruh) but not with laughing (which is haram), so the equation is partial.
This shows how the evidence is interpreted to reach the specific hukm level. The consensus (ijmaʿ) is the stronger proof here.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih. Some other schools may consider it merely khilaf al-awla (contrary to better choice) rather than makruh, but the Hanafi school explicitly states karahah.
Understand that the ruling is not unanimous across schools. The Hanafi position is based on the textual evidence and consensus, which other schools may weigh differently.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi is makruh tanzih, as concluded by later Hanafi authorities like al-Dirayah and the author. There is no internal Hanafi dispute.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

This lesson covers the ruling on cracking the fingers during prayer, defined as pressing or stretching them until a sound is made. The author states that there is consensus (ijmaʿ) in the Hanafi school that it is disliked (makruh). He supports this with two hadith: one from Ibn Majah (though with a weak chain due to al-Harith) prohibiting it, and another from Sahal ibn Muʿadh equating it with laughing and looking around in prayer. The text ends abruptly with 'perhaps' (walal), suggesting further discussion on the next page. The ruling is clear: cracking fingers is disliked; the hadith, though not the strongest, is supported by the consensus. The practical ruling: it is unnecessary noise and distraction, so it should be avoided.

Ruling on Cracking Knuckles and Interlocking Fingers in Prayer
Ruling with proof on cracking and interlocking fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cracking knuckles and interlocking fingers in prayer, identify the evidence, and distinguish between the ruling in prayer, while waiting, and outside prayer.

Core Idea
Cracking knuckles and interlocking fingers in prayer are makruh tahrimi; same for waiting or walking to prayer.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings on two common fidgeting acts during prayer: cracking knuckles (farqa' al-asabi') and interlocking fingers (tashbik). The author, Ibn Nujaym, discusses the evidence, including hadith and consensus, and clarifies the degree of dislike (tahrimi vs tanzihi) based on context. Understanding this helps maintain proper khushu' in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling in prayer
First, note that the text discusses two acts: cracking knuckles and interlocking fingers. In prayer, both are ruled makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). The prayer is valid but sinful.
The text explicitly states 'كراهة الفرقعة تحريمية' for prayer.
2
Examine the dalil
The primary evidence is a hadith: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are praying' (Ibn Majah). Although the hadith has a weak chain, it is supported by other narrations and consensus (ijma') cited in al-Dirayah. The prohibition indicates tahrimi per Hanafi usul.
The text also cites a hadith from Sahl ibn Mu'adh equating cracking with laughter, but notes laughter invalidates prayer while cracking does not.
3
Determine the degree of karahah and reasoning
The dislike is tahrimi because the prohibition is explicit and it is a form of fidgeting (abath). Outside prayer, if done without need it is also tahrimi; if for a need (e.g., relieving joints) it becomes tanzihi.
The commentator reconciles a statement from al-Hidayah that fidgeting outside prayer is haram, interpreting it as makruh tahrimi.
4
Apply the ruling to related contexts
The ruling extends to the one waiting for prayer in the mosque or walking to the mosque, based on a hadith prohibiting cracking while waiting or walking. They are considered in the state of prayer.
Interlocking fingers is ruled the same as cracking, with the same degree of dislike.
5
Distinguish from fidgeting outside prayer
Outside prayer, if done as mere fidgeting (abath) without need, it is also makruh tahrimi. If done for a legitimate need (e.g., relieving stiff joints), it is only makruh tanzihi.
The text mentions that al-Allamah al-Halabi did not find a clear ruling for outside prayer from earlier scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفرقعة الأصابع) وهو غمزها أو مدها حتى تصوت ونقل في الدراية الإجماع على كراهتها فيها، ومن السنة ما رواه ابن ماجه مرفوعاً «لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت تصلي ) (۳) لكنه معلول بالحارث . وروى سهل بن معاذ رفعه الضاحك في الصلاة والملتفت و المفرقع أصابعه بمنزلة واحدة»(٤) ولعل المراد التساوي في المعصية وإلا فالضحك مبطل .لها. وينبغي أن تكون كراهة الفرقعة تحريمية للنهي الوارد في ذلك ولأنها من أفراد العبث بخلاف الفرقعة خارج الصلاة لغير حاجة ولو لإراحة المفاصل فإنها تنزيهية على القول بالكراهة كما في المجتبي أنه كرهها كثير من الناس لأنها من الشيطان بالحديث اهـ . لكن لمالم يكن فيها خارجها نهي لم تكن تحريمية كما أسلفناه قريباً، والحق في المجتبي المنتظر للصلاة والماشي إليها بمن في الصلاة في كراهتها وروى في ذلك حديثاً أنه نهى أن يفرقع الرجل أصابعه وهو جالس في المسجد ينتظر الصلاة وفي رواية وهو يمشي إليها. وأشار المصنف إلى كراهة تشبيك الأصابع وهو أن يدخل إحدى أصابع يديه بين أصابع الأخرى في الصلاة كما صرح به في المحيط وغيره أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما مرفوعاً إذا توضأ أحدكم فأحسن وضوءه ثم خرج إلى المسجد فلا يشبك بين يديه فإنه في الصلاة» (۱) ونقل في الدراية إجماع العلماء على كراهته فيها . ثم يظهر أيضاً أنها تحريمية للنهي المذكور وظاهره الكراهة أيضاً حالة السعي إلى الصلاة فإذا كان منتظراً لها بالأولى. وذكر العلامة الحلبي أنه لم يقف على حكمه خارج الصلاة لمشايخنا، والظاهر أنه في غير هذين الموضعين لا للعبث ليس بمكروه ولو لإراحة الأصابع وإن كان على سبيل العبث يكره تنزيهاً .اهـ. وقد قدمنا عن الهداية أن العبث خارج الصلاة حرام وحملناه على كراهة التحريم فينبغي أن يكون العبث خارجها لغير حاجة كذلك
His statement: (And cracking the fingers) which is pressing or stretching them until they produce sound. It is narrated in al-Dirayah that there is consensus on its dislike in prayer. From the Sunna is what Ibn Majah narrated as marfu': 'Do not crack your fingers while you are praying,' but it is defective due to al-Harith. Sahl ibn Mu'adh narrated as marfu': 'The one who laughs in prayer, the one who looks around, and the one who cracks his fingers are all equal.' Perhaps the meaning is equality in sin, otherwise laughter invalidates prayer. It should be that the dislike of cracking is tahrimi due to the prohibition contained therein and because it is a type of fidgeting, unlike cracking outside prayer without need; even for relieving joints, it is tanzihi according to the view of dislike, as in al-Mujtaba: many people disliked it because it is from Shaytan according to a hadith. But as long as there is no prohibition outside prayer, it is not tahrimi, as we have mentioned recently. The correct view in al-Mujtaba is that the one waiting for prayer and the one walking to it are like the one in prayer regarding its dislike, and a hadith is narrated on that: he prohibited a man from cracking his fingers while sitting in the mosque waiting for prayer, and in another narration, while walking to it. The author indicated the dislike of interlocking fingers, which is inserting one finger of the hand between the other hand's fingers in prayer, as explicitly stated in al-Muhit and others from Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and others as marfu': 'When one of you performs wudu and does it well, then goes out to the mosque, let him not interlock his hands, for he is in prayer.' Consensus of scholars on its dislike in prayer is narrated in al-Dirayah. Then it appears also that it is tahrimi due to the mentioned prohibition, and its apparent meaning is dislike also in the state of walking to prayer, so for the one waiting it is more so. Al-Allamah al-Halabi mentioned that he did not come across a ruling for it outside prayer from our scholars, and the apparent is that in other than these two situations, if not for fidgeting, it is not disliked, and if for fidgeting it is disliked tanzihan. End. And we have previously mentioned from al-Hidayah that fidgeting outside prayer is haram, and we interpreted it as karahah tahrimiyyah, so fidgeting outside prayer without need should be likewise.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the rulings on cracking knuckles (farqa' al-asabi') and interlocking fingers (tashbik) during prayer. Cracking knuckles in prayer is considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) due to explicit prophetic prohibitions, even though one hadith is weak. The text analogizes it to fidgeting (abath) and notes that laughter invalidates prayer, but cracking does not. Outside prayer, if done for a purpose like relieving joints, it is only makruh tanzih; if done as mere fidgeting, it is also makruh tahrimi. For interlocking fingers, the ruling extends to the one waiting for prayer or walking to the mosque, as they are considered in the state of prayer. The consensus is that it is disliked, and the preferred view is that it is tahrimi in those situations. Outside these contexts, it is not disliked unless done as fidgeting. The commentary also reconciles a statement from al-Hidayah that fidgeting outside prayer is haram, interpreting it as makruh tahrimi.

Key Terms
فرقعة الأصابع (farqaʿat al-aṣābiʿ): Cracking knuckles by pressing or stretching fingers to produce sound.
تشبيك الأصابع (tashbīk al-aṣābiʿ): Interlocking fingers of one hand with the other.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, cracking knuckles and interlocking fingers during prayer are makruh tahrimi, and the same applies to those waiting or walking to prayer.

Ruling on Placing Hands on Hips During Prayer
Examines the ruling on placing hands on hips in prayer and its interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on takhassur during prayer, identify the evidence (dalil), and explain why it is classified as makruh tahrimi.

Core Idea
Placing hands on hips in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it resembles the people of Hell.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on a specific posture: placing hands on the waist (takhassur) during prayer. While it may seem like a minor act, the scholars derived a strong prohibition based on textual evidence and wisdom. Understanding this ruling helps refine one's prayer etiquette and awareness of prohibited actions.

Guided Path
1
Define the act (takhassur)
Identify the correct definition: placing the hand on the waist above the hipbones. This is supported by the majority of linguists, jurists, and hadith scholars.
Another interpretation (leaning on a staff) is mentioned but the correct one is as above.
2
State the ruling
The text concludes that it is makruh tahrimi in prayer. Outside prayer, it is also makruh but may be tanzihi.
The apparent ruling is tahrimi due to the explicit prohibition.
3
Identify the dalil
The evidence is a hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud: the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited it. This is a nakirah (unspecified) prohibition indicating tahrim according to Hanafi usul.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but the prohibition is clear.
4
Explain the wisdom (hikmah)
The act resembles the posture of the people of Hell, as narrated by Ibn Hibban. It is also associated with the actions of Jews, Christians, and the arrogant.
This reinforces the prohibition as it is not befitting for prayer.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi discussion
There is a statement in al-Hidayah that is criticized in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that it is makruh tahrimi.
The author of al-Bahr favors tahrimi based on the prohibition and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتخصر) وهو وضع اليد على الخاصرة وهي ما فوق الطفطفة والشراسيف - كذا في المغرب - لنهيه لله عنه كما في سنن أبي داود وهذا التفسير هو الصحيح وبه قال الجمهور من أهل اللغة والفقه والحديث ورد مفسراً هكذا عن ابن عمر كما في السنن . وحكمته أنه في الصلاة راحة أهل النار كما رواه ابن حبان في صحيحه. قال ابن حبان : يعني فعل اليهود والنصارى في صلاتهم وهم أهل النار لا أن لهم راحة في النار أو أنه فعل المتكبرين ولا يليق بالصلاة، أو أنه فعل الشيطان حتى قيل إن إبليس اهبط من لذلك فلهذا قال في المبسوط والمجتبى : ويكره التخصر خارج الصلاة أيضاً. والذي يظهر أنها تحريمية فيها للنهي المذكور. وقد فسر التخصر بغير هذا أيضاً منها أن يتوكأ في الصلاة قوله: (وقد قدمنا عن الهداية الخ) قال في النهر وأنت قد علمت أن ما في الهداية غير مسلم .اهـ. أي بما مر عن غاية
His statement: (And placing hands on hips) which is placing the hand on the waist above the hipbones and ribs - as in al-Mughrib - due to the prohibition from Allah as in Sunan Abi Dawud. This interpretation is correct and the majority of linguists, jurists, and hadith scholars hold it, and it is interpreted thus from Ibn Umar as in the Sunan. Its wisdom is that it is in prayer the resting of the people of Hell, as narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. Ibn Hibban said: It means the action of the Jews and Christians in their prayer, and they are the people of Hell; not that they have rest in Hell, or it is the action of the arrogant and is not befitting for prayer, or it is the action of Shaytan, even it is said that Iblis was cast down for that. Therefore, it is said in al-Mabsut and al-Mujtaba: It is disliked to place hands on hips outside prayer as well. And what appears is that it is tahrimi in prayer due to the mentioned prohibition. It has also been interpreted differently, including leaning on a staff in prayer. His statement: (And we have previously mentioned from al-Hidayah etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And you have known that what is in al-Hidayah is not accepted. End. Meaning by what passed from Ghayah.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on placing hands on hips (takhassur) during prayer. The correct definition is placing the hand on the waist, as supported by the majority of scholars and confirmed by a hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud. The wisdom behind the prohibition is that it resembles the posture of the people of Hell, or the actions of Jews, Christians, and the arrogant, or even the posture of Iblis. Hence, it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in prayer, and also disliked outside prayer according to al-Mabsut and al-Mujtaba. The text also mentions another interpretation of takhassur as leaning on a staff. The final note references a disagreement with a statement in al-Hidayah, but the relied-upon position is that it is tahrimi in prayer.

Key Terms
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing the hand on the waist, specifically on the hip area above the hipbones and ribs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is the posture of the Jews and Christians in prayer.
C) It helps maintain concentration.
D) It is a recommended sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Placing hands on the waist during prayer is makruh tahrimi due to explicit prohibition and resemblance to the actions of the people of Hell.

Ruling on Cracking Knuckles in Prayer and Outside
Explains the ruling of cracking knuckles with evidence from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cracking knuckles during prayer and outside, apply the distinction between makruh tahrimi and tanzih, and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Cracking knuckles in prayer is makruh tahrimi; outside it is tanzih unless for need.

The act of cracking one's knuckles may seem trivial, but Islamic law gives it a nuanced ruling depending on context. In this lesson, we examine a passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that explains why the dislike is prohibitive during prayer but only permissible dislike outside, and we trace the evidence from a hadith prohibition. Understanding this distinction deepens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists derive rulings from zanni proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses al-farqa'a (cracking knuckles). Determine the hukm level for two scenarios: during prayer and outside prayer.
The text explicitly states the ruling for inside prayer as makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the hadith cited: the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade a man from cracking his fingers while sitting in the mosque waiting for prayer, and while walking to it.
The hadith is narrated with two wordings, both indicating prohibition.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The explicit prohibition (نهى) in the hadith, combined with the Hanafi usul rule that a zanni proof indicates makruh tahrimi, leads to the ruling. The text also notes that cracking knuckles is a form of frivolity (عبث).
Since the hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), the ruling is makruh tahrimi, not haram.
4
Distinguish the rulings for different contexts
Outside prayer: if done without need, it is makruh tanzih; if for a need like relaxing joints, it is mubah. The text explains there is no prohibition outside prayer, so it cannot be tahrimi.
The waiting person and the one walking to prayer are treated like the one in prayer due to the hadith's scope.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position: inside prayer – makruh tahrimi; outside prayer without need – makruh tanzih; with need – mubah.
Some scholars consider even in prayer as tanzih, but the text declares the correct view (الحق) is tahrimi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبغي أن تكون كراهة الفرقعة تحريمية للنهي الوارد في ذلك ولأنها من أفراد العبث بخلاف الفرقعة خارج الصلاة لغير حاجة ولو لإراحة المفاصل فإنها تنزيهية على القول بالكراهة كما في المجتبي أنه كرهها كثير من الناس لأنها من الشيطان بالحديث اهـ . لكن لمالم يكن فيها خارجها نهي لم تكن تحريمية كما أسلفناه قريباً، والحق في المجتبي المنتظر للصلاة والماشي إليها بمن في الصلاة في كراهتها وروى في ذلك حديثاً أنه نهى أن يفرقع الرجل أصابعه وهو جالس في المسجد ينتظر الصلاة وفي رواية وهو يمشي إليها.
It should be that the dislike of cracking knuckles is prohibitive due to the prohibition narrated regarding that and because it is a type of frivolity, unlike cracking knuckles outside prayer without need, even for relaxing joints, for it is permissible dislike according to the view of dislike, as in al-Mujtaba that many people disliked it because it is from the devil according to the hadith. But since there is no prohibition outside it, it is not prohibitive as we mentioned recently. And the correct view in al-Mujtaba is that the one waiting for prayer and the one walking to it is like the one in prayer in its dislike, and it is narrated in that regard a hadith that he forbade a man to crack his fingers while sitting in the mosque waiting for prayer, and in another narration while walking to it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on cracking knuckles (الفرقعة). The author states that the dislike of this action during prayer is makruh tahrimi (prohibitive dislike) because of the explicit prohibition in the hadith and because it is a form of frivolous play. However, if done outside prayer for a genuine need such as relaxing joints, it is only makruh tanzih (permissible dislike) according to the view that it is disliked at all. The reason is that there is no explicit prohibition for outside prayer. The text also clarifies that the one waiting for prayer or walking to the mosque is considered like the one in prayer regarding the dislike, as evidenced by the hadith prohibiting cracking fingers in the mosque while waiting or walking to prayer.

Key Terms
الفرقعة (al-farqa'a): Cracking the knuckles or joints of the fingers by pulling or twisting them.
كراهة تحريمية (karāha taḥrīmiyya): A dislike that is close to prohibition; sin if done, but not invalidating.
كراهة تنزيهية (karāha tanzīhiyya): A disliked act that is not sinful; better to avoid but not haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Cracking knuckles in prayer is makruh tahrimi because of the explicit prohibition in a hadith; outside prayer, it is only makruh tanzih without need, and mubah with need.

Ruling on Interlocking Fingers in Prayer and Outside
Explains the dislike of interlocking fingers with hadith and scholarly consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on interlocking fingers in and outside prayer, distinguish between prohibitive and merely disliked contexts, and identify the key hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Interlocking fingers is prohibited during prayer and walking to it, but outside it depends on intention.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on interlocking fingers (تشفيك الأصابع). You will learn how a single action can carry different rulings based on context — from prohibitive dislike in prayer to permissible relaxation outside. The primary evidence is a hadith forbidding the act for one walking to the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its contexts
The act is interlocking fingers (tashbīk al-aṣābiʿ). Three contexts: (1) during prayer, (2) while walking to or waiting for prayer, (3) outside these.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Read the hadith: “When one of you performs ablution… then goes out to the mosque, let him not interlace his fingers, for he is in prayer.” Note that the prohibition covers both the prayer and the walk.
Narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting the prohibition
The imperative ‘lā yushabbik’ indicates wujub of avoidance. Combined with scholarly consensus, the Hanafis classify it as makruh tahrimi (prohibitive dislike) in prayer and walking.
The principle: al-amr yufīdu al-wujūb, and here the command is to avoid.
4
Distinguish the ruling outside prayer
Outside, it is not disliked if done for a need (e.g., relaxation). If done frivolously, it becomes makruh tanzih (lightly disliked). This distinction depends on intention.
Al-Halabi noted he did not find a clear ruling from earlier scholars; the author derives this.
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some early Hanafis considered it only tanzih even in prayer; but the mufta bihi is tahrimi.
The majority view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى كراهة تشبيك الأصابع وهو أن يدخل إحدى أصابع يديه بين أصابع الأخرى في الصلاة كما صرح به في المحيط وغيره أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما مرفوعاً إذا توضأ أحدكم فأحسن وضوءه ثم خرج إلى المسجد فلا يشبك بين يديه فإنه في الصلاة» (۱) ونقل في الدراية إجماع العلماء على كراهته فيها . ثم يظهر أيضاً أنها تحريمية للنهي المذكور وظاهره الكراهة أيضاً حالة السعي إلى الصلاة فإذا كان منتظراً لها بالأولى. وذكر العلامة الحلبي أنه لم يقف على حكمه خارج الصلاة لمشايخنا، والظاهر أنه في غير هذين الموضعين لا للعبث ليس بمكروه ولو لإراحة الأصابع وإن كان على سبيل العبث يكره تنزيهاً .اهـ. وقد قدمنا عن الهداية أن العبث خارج الصلاة حرام وحملناه على كراهة التحريم فينبغي أن يكون العبث خارجها لغير حاجة كذلك
And the author pointed to the dislike of interlocking fingers, which is to insert one finger of the hands between the fingers of the other in prayer, as clarified in al-Muhit and others, Ahmad and Abu Dawud and others narrated it marfu': 'When one of you performs ablution and does it well, then goes out to the mosque, let him not interlace his fingers, for he is in prayer.' And it is narrated in al-Diraya the consensus of scholars on its dislike in prayer. Then it appears that it is prohibitive due to the mentioned prohibition, and apparent also is dislike during the walk to prayer, and if waiting for it, even more so. Al-Halabi mentioned that he did not find its ruling outside prayer from our scholars, and the apparent is that outside these two situations, if not frivolous, it is not disliked; if to relax fingers, and if done in a frivolous way, it is disliked tanzihan. And we have previously cited from al-Hidayah that frivolity outside prayer is haram, and we interpreted it as prohibitive dislike, so it should be that frivolity outside it without need is likewise.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on interlocking fingers (تشبيك الأصابع) during prayer and related contexts. The author begins by citing the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade interlocking fingers for one who has performed ablution and is walking to the mosque, stating that he is considered in a state of prayer. The text reports a consensus among scholars on its dislike within the prayer. It further determines that this dislike is prohibitive (makruh tahrimi) due to the explicit prohibition. The same applies when walking to prayer, and more so when waiting. Outside these two contexts, if done without frivolity (e.g., to relax fingers), it is not disliked; if done frivolously, it is makruh tanzih. The author also references a discussion from al-Hidayah that frivolity outside prayer is haram, which is interpreted as makruh tahrimi, thus extending that ruling.

Key Terms
تشبيك الأصابع (tashbīk al-aṣābiʿ): Interlocking the fingers of one hand with the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Makruh tahrimi
B) Makruh tanzih
C) Mubah
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
Interlocking fingers is prohibited in prayer and when heading to or waiting for it; outside, it is only disliked if done frivolously.

Ruling on Placing Hand on Hip (Takhassur) in Prayer
Defines takhassur and expands its meanings with detailed rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define takhassur, state its primary ruling (makruh tahrimi in prayer), and distinguish between its various interpretations and their respective rulings.

Core Idea
Placing hands on hips in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it resembles the arrogant and is prohibited; other meanings of takhassur carry different rulings.

In this lesson, we examine the term التخصر (takhassur) as defined by the Hanafi school. While the most common understanding is placing the hand on the hip, the classical scholars have expanded its meaning to include other actions that shorten or modify the prayer. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the disliked ruling. We will trace the primary definition, its evidence, and then explore the expanded interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition and its evidence
Locate the primary meaning of takhassur: placing the hand on the hip. Note the evidence from the Prophet's prohibition (hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud).
The majority of scholars agree on this definition.
2
Understand the ruling (hukm) derived from the prohibition
Apply the Hanafi usul principle: the prohibition (نهي) indicates makruh tahrimi. Confirm this with the text's statement 'which appears is prohibitive'.
The wisdom mentioned: it is the comfort of the people of Hellfire or action of the arrogant.
3
Explore the expanded meanings of takhassur
List the other interpretations: leaning on a stick, shortening a surah, omitting the verse of prostration, or shortening the prayer itself. Each has its own ruling depending on context.
These expansions come from the same term but apply to different actions.
4
For each expanded meaning, determine the degree of dislike
Analyze conditions: leaning in fard is makruh without necessity, not in nafl. Shortening recitation is makruh tahrimi if it leaves a wajib (e.g., less than three verses), otherwise not disliked. Omitting verse of prostration is makruh. Shortening prayer integrals is tahrimi if it leaves a wajib, tanzih if it leaves a sunna.
The ruling varies based on whether a wajib or sunna is omitted.
5
Summarize the overall Hanafi position
State the mufta bihi: the primary act of hand on hip is makruh tahrimi in prayer, makruh tanzih outside. Other interpretations follow their own rules as detailed.
This demonstrates the systematic application of usul to furu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتخصر) وهو وضع اليد على الخاصرة وهي ما فوق الطفطفة والشراسيف - كذا في المغرب - لنهيه صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه كما في سنن أبي داود وهذا التفسير هو الصحيح وبه قال الجمهور من أهل اللغة والفقه والحديث ورد مفسراً هكذا عن ابن عمر كما في السنن . وحكمته أنه في الصلاة راحة أهل النار كما رواه ابن حبان في صحيحه. قال ابن حبان : يعني فعل اليهود والنصارى في صلاتهم وهم أهل النار لا أن لهم راحة في النار أو أنه فعل المتكبرين ولا يليق بالصلاة، أو أنه فعل الشيطان حتى قيل إن إبليس اهبط من لذلك فلهذا قال في المبسوط والمجتبى : ويكره التخصر خارج الصلاة أيضاً. والذي يظهر أنها تحريمية فيها للنهي المذكور. وقد فسر التخصر بغير هذا أيضاً منها أن يتوكأ في الصلاة على عصا، ومنها أن يختصر السورة فيقرأ من أولها آية أو آيتين ومنها أن يختصرها فيقرأ آخرها، ومنها أن يحذف آية السجدة ومنها أن يختصر صلاته فلا يتم حدودها، ولا شك في كراهة الاتكاء في الفرض لغير ضرورة كما صرحوا به لا في النفل على الأصح كما في المجتبي، وأما الاختصار في القراءة فإن أخل بواجب بأن نقص عن ثلاث آيات كان مكروهاً كراهة تحريم لترك بعض الواجب إلا فلا. وقد صرح أصحاب الفتاوى بأن الصحيح أنه لا تكره القراءة من آخر السورة وقد صرحوا بكراهة قراءة السورة وترك آية السجدة في بابها، وأما اختصار الصلاة بحيث لا يتم حدودها فإن لزم منه ترك واجب كره تحريماً وإن أخل بسنة كره تنزيهاً. هذا ما تقتضيه القواعد والله سبحانه الموفق للصواب.
His saying: (al-takhassur) and it is placing the hand on the hip, which is above the flank and the ribs - thus in al-Mughrib - because of his (peace be upon him) prohibition of it as in Sunan Abi Dawud. And this interpretation is the correct one, and the majority of linguists, jurists, and hadith scholars say it, and it is narrated as such from Ibn Umar. And its wisdom is that it is the comfort of the people of Hellfire in prayer as narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. Ibn Hibban said: meaning the action of Jews and Christians in their prayer, and they are the people of Hellfire, not that they have comfort in Hellfire; or it is the action of the arrogant and is not befitting for prayer, or it is the action of the devil until it was said that Iblis was sent down because of it. Therefore it is said in al-Mabsut and al-Mujtaba: And it is disliked outside prayer as well. And what appears is that it is prohibitive within it due to the mentioned prohibition. And takhassur has also been interpreted otherwise, including leaning in prayer on a stick, and to shorten the surah by reciting one or two verses from its beginning, and to shorten it by reciting its end, and to omit the verse of prostration, and to shorten his prayer by not completing its integrals. There is no doubt about the dislike of leaning in obligatory prayer without necessity as they stated, not in supererogatory on the correct view. As for shortening in recitation, if it violates an obligatory element like reducing to less than three verses, it is makruh tahrimi due to leaving part of the obligatory, otherwise not. The fatwa authors have stated that the correct view is that reciting from the end of a surah is not disliked, and they have stated the dislike of reciting a surah while omitting the verse of prostration in its place. As for shortening the prayer to not complete its integrals, if it necessitates leaving an obligatory element, it is disliked prohibitively; if it leaves a sunna, it is disliked tanzihan. This is what the principles require, and Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines and rules on التخصر (placing hands on hips) in prayer. The primary meaning is placing the hand on the hip, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited it, making it makruh tahrimi in prayer. The wisdom is that it resembles the comfort of the people of Hellfire or the actions of Jews and Christians. The text also mentions that it is disliked outside prayer as well, but the degree is not specified as tahrimi. Other interpretations of takhassur include leaning on a stick, reciting only part of a surah (which may be makruh if it omits a wajib), omitting the verse of prostration, or shortening the prayer so that its integrals are incomplete. The author distinguishes between obligatory and supererogatory prayers, and between violation of wajib (makes tahrimi) and sunna (makes tanzih). This is a detailed ruling showing the application of principles.

Key Terms
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing the hand on the hip (خاصرة) or in a broader sense, any shortening of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on placing hands on hips (takhassur) in obligatory prayer according to the text?
A) Makruh tahrimi
B) Makruh tanzih
C) Mubah
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
The primary meaning of takhassur is placing hand on hip, which is makruh tahrimi in prayer, but the term also covers other actions with varying degrees of dislike.

Ruling on Looking Around (Iltifat) in Prayer
Discusses the ruling of turning the face away from qibla during prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on turning the face from qibla in prayer and identify the conditions that make it disliked or permissible.

Core Idea
Turning the face from the qibla in prayer is makruh tahrimi, but does not invalidate the prayer.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the ruling on iltifat—turning the face away from the qibla—during prayer. The author cites strong hadith evidence and distinguishes between turning the face (disliked) versus moving only the eyes (permissible). Understanding this distinction is essential for proper prayer according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is iltifat? What is the hukm level (makruh tahrimi) and why?
The act is defined as turning the face away from the qibla.
2
Locate the primary dalil
What hadiths are cited and what do they prove about prohibition?
Bukhari and Tirmidhi hadiths are used to establish the ruling.
3
Distinguish conditions
When is iltifat disliked? When is it not? How does moving the eyes differ?
With excuse (e.g., illness) it is not disliked; moving eyes is never disliked.
4
Note the evidence for non-invalidation
How do the hadiths show that iltifat does not break the prayer?
The hadith calls it a 'snatch' from prayer but still refers to it as prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (والالتفات) لما رواه البخاري عن عائشة الله عنها قالت: سألت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الالتفات في الصلاة فقال : هو اختلاس يختلسه الشيطان من صلاة العبد. وروى الترمذي وصححه عن أنس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إياك والالتفات في الصلاة فإن الالتفات في الصلاة ،هلكة فإن كان لا بد ففي التطوع لا في الفريضة»(٢). ثم المذكور في عامة الكتب لأن الالتفات المكروه هو تحويل وجهه عن القبلة وممن صرح به صاحب البدائع والنهاية والغاية والتبيين وفتح القدير والمجتبي والكافي وشرح المجمع، وقيده في الغاية بأن يكون لغير عذر، أما تحويل الوجه لعذر فغير مكروه. وينبغي أن تكون تحريمية كما هو ظاهر الأحاديث قالوا : وإنما كره لغير عذر لأنه انحراف عن القبلة ببعض بدنه ولو انحرف عنها بجميع بدنه فسدت فإن انحرف ببعض بدنه كره كالعمل القليل فإنه مكروه لأن كثيره مفسد ويدل لعدم فسادها بهذا الالتفات قوله في الحديث يختلسها الشيطان من صلاة العبد فإنه سماها صلاة معه. وإنما لم يكره للعذر الحديث مسلم عن جابر اشتکی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلينا وراءه وهو قاعد فالتفت إلينا فرآنا قياماً فأشار إلينا فقعدنا وقد صرحوا بأن التفات البصر يمنة ويسرة من غير تحويل الوجه أصلاً غير مكروه مطلقاً والأولى تركه لغير حاجة، والظاهر أن فعله عليه السلام إياه كان لحاجة تفقد أحوال المقتدين به مع ما فيه من بيان الجواز وإلا فهو كان ينظر من خلفه كما ينظر أمامه كما في الصحيحين. وقد خالف صاحب السروجي قوله : (وهي ما فوق الطفطفة والشراسيف) الطفطفة أطراف الخاصرة، والشراسيف أطراف الضلع الذي يشرف على البطن نهاية عن المغرب. قوله : ( والأولى تركه لغير (حاجة أي فيكون مكروهاً تنزيهاً كما هو مرجع خلاف الأولى كما مر وبه صرح في النهر، وفي الزيلعي وشرح الملتقى للباقاني أنه مباح لأنه لما كان يلاحظ أصحابه
His saying (al-iltifat) because of what al-Bukhari narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: I asked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) about looking around in prayer, and he said: 'It is a snatch that the devil snatches from the prayer of the servant.' And al-Tirmidhi narrated and authenticated from Anas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Beware of looking around in prayer, for looking around in prayer is a loss. If it must be, then in supererogatory prayer, not in obligatory.' Then it is mentioned in most books that the disliked iltifat is turning one's face away from the qibla, and those who stated this include the authors of al-Bada'i, al-Nihaya, al-Ghaya, al-Tabyin, Fath al-Qadir, al-Mujtaba, al-Kafi, and Sharh al-Majma'. And it is qualified in al-Ghaya that it is for an excuse; turning the face for an excuse is not disliked. And it should be prohibitive as is apparent from the hadiths. They said: It is only disliked without excuse because it is a deviation from the qibla with part of the body; if he deviates with all of his body, it invalidates; if he deviates with part of it, it is disliked, like a small action, for a large action invalidates. And the evidence that it does not invalidate is his saying in the hadith 'he snatches it from the prayer of the servant' for he called it prayer along with it. And it was not disliked for an excuse due to the hadith of Muslim from Jabir that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was ill and they prayed behind him while he was sitting, and he turned to them and saw them standing, so he gestured to them to sit. And they have stated that turning the eyes right and left without turning the face at all is not disliked at all, and it is better to leave it without need. And the apparent is that his action (peace be upon him) was for the need of checking on his followers and also to show permissibility; otherwise, he used to see behind him as he sees in front of him as in the two Sahihs. And the author of al-Suruji differed in his saying: (and it is above the flank and the ribs) the flank is the ends of the hip, and the ribs are the ends of the rib that overhangs the belly, finally from al-Mughrib. His saying: (and it is better to leave it without need) meaning it is makruh tanzihi as the reference to 'better to leave' as passed, and it is stated in al-Nahr, and in al-Zaylai and Sharh al-Multaqa for al-Baqani that it is permissible because he was observing his companions.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on looking around (الالتفات) in prayer. The author defines the disliked iltifat as turning the face away from the qibla, supported by hadiths from Bukhari and Tirmidhi. The ruling is that it is makruh tahrimi for obligatory prayer, and if done without excuse, it is sinful but does not invalidate the prayer, as indicated by the hadith calling it a 'snatch' but still prayer. If it is for an excuse, it is not disliked, as the Prophet himself did it when ill. Moving only the eyes (without turning the face) is not disliked at all, though it is better to avoid without need. The text also mentions a disagreement from al-Suruji but the main ruling remains.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifāt): Turning the face away from the qibla during prayer, as opposed to moving only the eyes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on turning the face away from the qibla in obligatory prayer without an excuse?
A) Invalidates prayer
B) Makruh tahrimi
C) Makruh tanzih
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Turning the face from qibla in obligatory prayer without excuse is makruh tahrimi but does not invalidate the prayer.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Leaning, Shortening Recitation, and Omitting Sajda
Ruling statements on disliked actions in prayer from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on disliked acts in prayer (leaning, shortening recitation, omitting sajda, and shortening the prayer) and classify them as makruh tahrimi or tanzihi based on whether a wajib or sunnah is omitted.

Core Idea
The degree of dislike depends on what is omitted: leaving a wajib makes it makruh tahrimi; leaving a sunnah makes it makruh tanzihi.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing several disliked acts in prayer. The author explains the nuanced rulings for 'shortening' (ikhtisar) in recitation and in the prayer itself, as well as leaning (ittika') and omitting the verse of prostration. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for performing prayer correctly, as it affects the degree of sin and recommended repetition.

Guided Path
1
List the acts discussed
Identify the four acts: leaning, shortening recitation, omitting sajda verse, and shortening the prayer limits.
2
Determine the default ruling for each act
Note that leaning is disliked only in fard without necessity, and not in nafl. Shortening recitation and prayer varies. Omitting sajda verse is disliked.
The fatwa authors clarify that reading from the end of a surah is not disliked.
3
Apply the wajib/sunnah criterion
If the shortening causes a wajib to be omitted (e.g., reciting less than three verses), it becomes makruh tahrimi. If only a sunnah is omitted, it is makruh tanzihi.
What are the wajibat in recitation? At least three short verses after Fatiha is wajib.
4
Recognize the consequence
Makruh tahrimi is sinful but does not invalidate the prayer; makruh tanzihi is less severe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها مع الفاتحة على عصا، ومنها أن يختصر السورة فيقرأ من أولها آية أو آيتين ومنها أن يختصرها فيقرأ آخرها، ومنها أن يحذف آية السجدة ومنها أن يختصر صلاته فلا يتم حدودها، ولا شك في كراهة الاتكاء في الفرض لغير ضرورة كما صرحوا به لا في النفل على الأصح كما في المجتبي، وأما الاختصار في القراءة فإن أخل بواجب بأن نقص عن ثلاث آيات كان مكروهاً كراهة تحريم لترك بعض الواجب إلا فلا. وقد صرح أصحاب الفتاوى بأن الصحيح أنه لا تكره القراءة من آخر السورة وقد صرحوا بكراهة قراءة السورة وترك آية السجدة في بابها، وأما اختصار الصلاة بحيث لا يتم حدودها فإن لزم منه ترك واجب كره تحريماً وإن أخل بسنة كره تنزيهاً. هذا ما تقتضيه القواعد والله سبحانه الموفق للصواب.
Book of Prayer, Chapter: What Nullifies Prayer and What is Disliked Therein. With al-Fatihah on a stick? And among them is to shorten the surah by reading from its beginning one or two verses, and among them is to shorten it by reading its end, and among them is to omit the verse of prostration, and among them is to shorten his prayer so that he does not complete its limits. There is no doubt about the dislike of leaning in obligatory prayer without necessity as they have stated, but not in supererogatory prayer according to the most sound view as in al-Mujtaba. As for shortening the recitation, if it violates an obligatory by reducing to less than three verses, it is makruh tahrimi because of abandoning part of the wajib, otherwise not. The authors of fatwas have stated that the correct view is that reading from the end of the surah is not disliked, and they have stated the dislike of reading the surah and leaving the verse of prostration in its proper place. As for shortening the prayer such that he does not complete its limits: if it leads to leaving a wajib, it is makruh tahrimi; if it violates a sunnah, it is makruh tanzihi. This is what the rules require, and Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding disliked acts in prayer. The author discusses several types of 'shortening' (ikhtisar). The first is shortening the recitation by reading only one or two verses from the beginning or end of a surah, which is disliked. Also disliked is omitting the verse of prostration (ayat al-sajdah) from a surah because it is a recommended act. Another type is shortening the prayer itself by not completing its boundaries, i.e., omitting some of its sunan or wajibat. The author then addresses leaning (ittika') in prayer: it is disliked in obligatory prayer without excuse, but not in nafl according to the preferred view. For shortening recitation, if it results in leaving a wajib (such as reciting less than three verses in a rakah), it is makruh tahrimi; otherwise it is merely tanzihi. The fatwa authors assert that reading from the end of a surah is not disliked, confirming that the earlier ruling applies only to reading from the beginning while omitting the middle. Shortening the prayer's limits is evaluated based on what is omitted: if a wajib is left, it is tahrimi; if only a sunnah, it is tanzihi. The passage concludes that these rulings follow legal principles.

Key Terms
اختصار (ikhtisar): Shortening, whether of recitation or of the prayer itself by omitting parts.
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrīmiyyah): A disliked act that is close to haram; requires repetition if done intentionally in prayer? Actually it is a prohibited dislike but does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does shortening the recitation (ikhtisar al-qira'ah) become makruh tahrimi?
A) When it involves reading from the end of the surah
B) When it reduces the recitation to less than three verses, thereby violating a wajib
C) When it omits the verse of prostration
D) When it is done in supererogatory prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between makruh tahrimi (leaving a wajib) and makruh tanzihi (leaving a sunnah) in prayer, and the ruling varies for each specific act.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

The Ruling on Turning Away (Iltifat) in Prayer: Disliked but Not Invalidating
Ruling on turning away in prayer with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on turning the face away from the qiblah during prayer, identify the proof from hadith, and distinguish between disliked and invalidating turning.

Core Idea
Turning the face alone in prayer is makruh tahrimi; only turning the entire chest invalidates the prayer.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between turning the face and turning the entire body in prayer. This lesson examines the proofs from the Sunnah that classify iltifat as a devilish snatching, and explains the threshold for nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is turning the face away from the qiblah during prayer without excuse. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) according to the author, based on the hadith warnings.
If there is an excuse (e.g., following the imam), it is not disliked.
2
Locate the dalil
Two ahadith are cited: from Bukhari (Aisha) where the Prophet says iltifat is 'a snatching by the devil', and from Tirmidhi (Anas) with the imperative 'Beware of turning away in prayer'.
The hadiths are ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for ruling on dislike.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's description of iltifat as 'snatching' indicates that it is detrimental but does not nullify the prayer (since he still called it 'prayer'). The imperative shows strong prohibition.
The hadith from Muslim (Jabir) shows that turning for an excuse is permissible.
4
Note the condition for nullification
Turning the face alone does not invalidate. Only when the entire body (chest) deviates from the qiblah does the prayer become batil.
The text states: 'ولو انحرف عنها بجميع بدنه فسدت'.
5
Identify exceptions
With a valid excuse (e.g., illness, following the imam), turning the face is not disliked.
The hadith of Jabir proves this.

الله عليه وسلم إياك والالتفات في الصلاة فإن الالتفات في الصلاة ،هلكة فإن كان لا بد ففي التطوع لا في الفريضة»(٢). ثم المذكور في عامة الكتب لأن الالتفات المكروه هو تحويل وجهه عن القبلة وممن صرح به صاحب البدائع والنهاية والغاية والتبيين وفتح القدير والمجتبي والكافي وشرح المجمع، وقيده في الغاية بأن يكون لغير عذر، أما تحويل الوجه لعذر فغير مكروه. وينبغي أن تكون تحريمية كما هو ظاهر الأحاديث قالوا : وإنما كره لغير عذر لأنه انحراف عن القبلة ببعض بدنه ولو انحرف عنها بجميع بدنه فسدت فإن انحرف ببعض بدنه كره كالعمل القليل فإنه مكروه لأن كثيره مفسد ويدل لعدم فسادها بهذا الالتفات قوله في الحديث يختلسها الشيطان من صلاة العبد فإنه سماها صلاة معه. وإنما لم يكره للعذر الحديث مسلم عن جابر اشتکی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلينا وراءه وهو قاعد فالتفت إلينا فرآنا قياماً فأشار إلينا فقعدنا وقد صرحوا بأن من خلفه كما ينظر أمامه كما في الصحيحين. وقد خالف صاحب السروجي قوله : (وهي ما فوق الطفطفة والشراسيف) الطفطفة أطراف الخاصرة، والشراسيف أطراف الضلع الذي يشرف على البطن نهاية عن المغرب. قوله : ( والأولى تركه لغير حاجة أي فيكون مكروهاً تنزيهاً كما هو مرجع خلاف الأولى كما مر وبه صرح في النهر، وفي الزيلعي وشرح الملتقى للباقاني أنه مباح لأنه لما كان يلاحظ أصحابه من ساعته الخلاصة عامة الكتب في الالتفات
