يحدث قبل الخروج من المسجد لا تبطل، ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية اشتراط أن ركناً وهو مستدبر لما صرحوا به من أن انكشاف العورة إنما يفسدها إذا لم يستتر من ساعته حتى أدى ركناً أما إذا سترها قبل أداء الركن فلا ، فكذا استقبال القبلة بجامع الشرطية والمكث قدر أداء الركن فيه خلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد؛ فأبو يوسف لا يجعله كأداء الركن، ومحمد جعله كما عرف. وذكر الشارح أنه يكره رفع بصره إلى السماء لقوله عليه السلام «ما بال أقوام يرفعون أبصارهم إلى السماء في الصلاة، لينتهن أو لتخطفن أبصارهم»(۱) وفي التجنيس : ويكره أن يميل أصابع يديه ورجليه عن القبلة لأنه مأمور بتوجيهها قال عليه السلام «فليوجه من أعضائه إلى القبلة ما استطاع». يؤدي في صلاته بموق عينيه ولعل المراد عند عدم الحاجة فلا ينافي ما هنا قوله : (وكأنه جمع الخ) قال في النهر: فيه بحث اهـ. وفي شرح نظم الكنز للعلامة المقدسي : لكن ظهر لي والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم أن مراد الخلاصة بتحويل الوجه المفسد تحويل جميعه عن القبلة وذلك يلزم منه تحويل الصدر لأن الوجه ليس بمستو بل فيه استدارة فإذا حول عن القبلة بأن أزيل بعضه عن مسامتتها كالجانب الأيمن منه بقي الجانب الأيسر منه مسامتاً فلا تفسد فإذا حول الجميع كان الصدر أيضاً محولاً فتفسد الصلاة ولهذا قالوا في باب استقبال القبلة : لا تفسد إلا بتحوله من المشارق إلى المغارب فليتأمل اهـ . قلت : ويشعر بذلك جعل الخانية الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه ولعل هذا مراد النهر بالبحث فيما قاله المؤلف قوله : ( ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية (الخ) كأنه لم ير فيه نقلاً صريحاً وقد رأيت في الحاوي
His saying (and turning away): because of what Bukhari narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: I asked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) about turning away in prayer, and he said: 'It is a snatching that the devil snatches from the prayer of the servant.' And Tirmidhi narrated and authenticated from Anas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Beware of turning away in prayer, for turning away in prayer is destruction. If it must be, then in voluntary prayer, not in obligatory.' Then what is mentioned in most books is that disliked turning away is turning one's face away from the qiblah, and those who stated this include the author of al-Bada'i', al-Nihayah, al-Ghayah, al-Tabyin, Fath al-Qadir, al-Mujtaba, al-Kafi, and Sharh al-Majma'. Al-Ghayah qualified it as being without excuse; turning the face for an excuse is not disliked. It should be makruh tahrimi as is apparent from the hadith. They said: It is disliked without excuse because it is a deviation from the qiblah with part of his body. If he deviates with his entire body, his prayer becomes invalid. So if he deviates with part of his body, it is disliked, like a small amount of action, which is disliked because its abundance invalidates. The evidence that this turning does not invalidate is the Prophet's saying in the hadith 'the devil snatches it from the prayer of the servant' because he called it prayer while it occurs. And it is not disliked when there is an excuse due to the hadith of Muslim from Jabir: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) became ill, so we prayed behind him while he was sitting, and he turned to us and saw us standing, so he gestured to us to sit down.' And they stated that moving the eyes right and left without turning the face at all is not disliked at all, but it is better to leave it when there is no need. It seems that his action (peace be upon him) was due to the need to check the conditions of those following him, along with indicating permissibility. Otherwise, he used to see behind him as he saw in front of him as in the two Sahihs. And al-Saruji differed in his saying (and it is what is above the tafṭafah and sharāsīf): al-ṭafṭafah is the sides of the loins, and al-sharāsīf are the ends of the rib that overhangs the belly, according to al-Mughrib. His saying (and it is better to leave it without need) meaning it would be makruh tanzihi, as it is the reference for khilaf al-awla as previously mentioned, and al-Nahr stated this clearly. In al-Zayla'i and Sharh al-Multaqa by al-Baqqani, it is permissible because he used to observe his companions from his [?]. The Khulasah (summary) of the general books on disliked turning made it invalidating, stating: 'If the praying person turns his face away from the qiblah without excuse, his prayer becomes invalid.' Similarly in al-Khaniyyah, where disliked turning is defined as turning part of the face from the qiblah. The more appropriate view is what is in the general books: that disliked turning includes turning all or part of the face. It is mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli that the dislike of turning with the face applies when he faces [the qiblah] immediately. Meaning, if he does not face immediately, it becomes invalid. It is as if he combined between the fatwas and the general books by interpreting the fatwas to apply when he does not face immediately, and the general books to apply when he faces immediately. And it is as if he considered that if he does not face immediately, it becomes many actions and invalidates, and if he faces immediately, it is a small action and is disliked. But this is far-fetched, because continuity on this small action does not make it many; rather, its abundance is turning his chest. And they have stated that invalidity occurs when turning the chest. It must be qualified by absence of excuse, as in Munyat al-Musalli, due to their explicit statement as mentioned that if he thought he had broken wudu and turned his back to the qiblah, then learned he had not before leaving the mosque, it does not invalidate. And the requirements of the school's legal principles: the condition that he performs a pillar while facing away, because they stated that uncovering the private parts only invalidates if he does not cover immediately until he performs a pillar; but if he covers before performing the pillar, it does not invalidate. Similarly, facing the qiblah, considering the common factor of conditionality, and staying for the duration of performing a pillar, there is a difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Abu Yusuf does not consider it like performing a pillar, while Muhammad considers it as known. And the commentator stated that it is disliked to raise his eyes to the sky, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'What is wrong with people who raise their eyes to the sky during prayer? Let they desist, or their eyesight will be snatched away.' And in al-Tajnis: It is disliked to incline the fingers of his hands and feet from the qiblah because he is commanded to direct them. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Let him direct from his limbs to the qiblah as much as he can.' He performs in his prayer with the corner of his eyes; perhaps the meaning is when there is no need, so it does not contradict what is here. His saying (and it is as if he combined) etc. Al-Nahr said: there is discussion here. In Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by al-Maqdisi: But it appears to me, and Allah knows best, that the meaning of al-Khulasah's 'invalidating turning of the face' is turning all of it from the qiblah, and that necessitates turning the chest because the face is not straight but has curvature. So if he turns from the qiblah by removing part of it from alignment, like the right side, the left side remains aligned, so it does not invalidate. If he turns all of it, the chest also turns, so prayer becomes invalid. For this reason they said in the chapter on facing the qiblah: 'It only invalidates if he turns from the east to the west.' So let it be pondered. I say: This is indicated by al-Khaniyyah's making disliked turning as turning part of his face, and perhaps this is what al-Nahr meant by discussion in what the author said. His saying (and the requirements of the school's legal principles) etc. It is as if he did not see an explicit text, and I have seen in al-Hawi...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the ruling of turning away (iltifat) in prayer. The author begins by citing two hadiths: one from Bukhari where the Prophet (peace be upon him) describes iltifat as a snatching by the devil, and another from Tirmidhi warning against it and stating that if it must be done, it should be in nafl, not fard. The author then clarifies the definition of disliked iltifat: according to most Hanafi books, it is turning the face away from the qiblah without excuse. If there is an excuse, it is not disliked. The author opines that it should be makruh tahrimi based on the hadiths. The reason for dislike without excuse is that it is a deviation from the qiblah with part of the body; if the entire body deviates, the prayer is invalidated. To show that this partial turn does not nullify prayer, the author points to the hadith calling it 'snatching from prayer', implying it is still prayer. Excused turning is supported by a hadith from Muslim where the Prophet turned to see his companions while leading prayer due to illness. Moving the eyes without turning the face is not disliked at all, though better avoided. The author then discusses a difference between al-Khulasah and other books: some say turning part of the face invalidates; others say only turning all the face invalidates. He resolves by explaining that if one does not immediately face the qiblah again, it becomes many actions and invalidates; otherwise, it is just disliked. He also notes that turning the chest invalidates. The author then mentions the disliked acts of raising eyes to the sky and tilting fingers and feet from the qiblah, citing relevant hadiths. Finally, he discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about whether remaining facing away for the duration of a pillar counts as performing a pillar. The passage concludes with a remark from al-Maqdisi clarifying that turning the entire face necessarily turns the chest and thus invalidates, while turning only part of the face does not. This summary reflects the passage's focus on the details of iltifat and related disliked acts.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifāt): Turning the face away from the qiblah during prayer, either partially or completely.
اختلاس (ikhtilās): A snatching or stealing; here it refers to the devil's theft of the reward of prayer through turning away.
تحريمية (taḥrīmiyyah): A level of dislike close to haram; the author says iltifat without excuse should be makruh tahrimi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does the commentator give that turning the face (iltifat) does not invalidate the prayer?
A) The hadith says 'it is destruction' so it must be invalid.
B) The Prophet called it 'snatching from prayer' implying the prayer continues.
C) The Quran explicitly allows turning.
D) There is no evidence; it is just a majority opinion.

Key Takeaway
Turning the face alone in prayer is makruh tahrimi, but prayer remains valid; only turning the entire body invalidates.

When Does Turning Become Invalidating? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Continuity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether prolonged turning invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether remaining facing away for the duration of a pillar counts as performing a pillar and invalidates the prayer.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf says remaining facing away for a pillar's duration is not like performing it; Muhammad says it is, leading to possible invalidation.

Within the Hanafi school, two great imams differ on the effect of prolonged turning away. This lesson analyzes their views and connects them to the broader principles of conditions (shurut) and pillars (arkan) in prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
If a person turns his face away and remains facing that direction for a duration equal to performing one rukn (e.g., reciting a short verse), does this count as performing that rukn while facing away, thereby invalidating the prayer?
The issue arises when the chest may or may not have turned; but here we assume only the face turned.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf does not consider staying facing away for the duration of a pillar as equivalent to performing the pillar. Therefore, the prayer remains valid (though the act is disliked).
This view is based on the principle that iltifat without chest movement is not a major deviation.
3
Identify Muhammad's view
Muhammad al-Shaybani considers that if one remains facing away for the time it takes to perform a rukn, it is as if he performed that rukn while facing away, which violates the condition of facing the qiblah. Thus, the prayer becomes invalid.
This is analogous to the rule of covering the private parts: if uncovered for a rukn's duration without covering, the prayer is invalid.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Yusuf, turning the face and remaining for a long time is still not invalid; according to Muhammad, it becomes invalid. This affects whether one must repeat the prayer.
The author notes that the more appropriate view (ashbah) is that turning the face alone is disliked and only turning the chest invalidates.
5
Relate to the rules of covering the private parts
The text draws a parallel: just as uncovering the private parts only invalidates if it continues for a rukn's duration without covering, similarly, facing away from qiblah (with the chest) for a rukn's duration invalidates.
This parallel helps understand the difference between the two imams' methodologies.

المكروه فجعله مفسداً وعبارته: ولو حول المصلي وجهه القبلة من غير عذر فسدت وكذا في الخانية وجعل فيها الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه عن القبلة والأشبه ما في عامة الكتب من أن الالتفات المكروه أعم من تحويل جميع الوجه أو بعضه. وذكر في منية المصلي أن كراهة الالتفات بالوجه فيما إذا استقبل من . يعني فلو لم يستقبل من ساعته فسدت وكأنه جمع بين ما في الفتاوى وبين ما في عامة الكتب بحمل ما في الفتاوى على ما إذا لم يستقبل من ساعته وحمل ما في العامة على ما إذا استقبل ساعته وكأنه ناظر إلى أنه إذا لم يستقبل من ساعته صار عملاً كثيراً فأفسدها، وإذا استقبل ساعته كان عملاً قليلاً فكره وهو بعيد فإن الاستدامة على هذا القليل لا يجعله كثيراً وإنما كثيره تحويل صدره، وقد صرحوا بالفساد عند تحويل الصدر. ولا بد من تقييده بعدم العذر كما في منية المصلي لتصريحهم كما سبق بأنه لو ظن أنه أحدث فاستدبر القبلة ثم علم أنه لم يحدث قبل الخروج من المسجد لا تبطل، ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية اشتراط أن ركناً وهو مستدبر لما صرحوا به من أن انكشاف العورة إنما يفسدها إذا لم يستتر من ساعته حتى أدى ركناً أما إذا سترها قبل أداء الركن فلا ، فكذا استقبال القبلة بجامع الشرطية والمكث قدر أداء الركن فيه خلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد؛ فأبو يوسف لا يجعله كأداء الركن، ومحمد جعله كما عرف. وذكر الشارح أنه يكره رفع بصره إلى السماء لقوله عليه السلام «ما بال أقوام يرفعون أبصارهم إلى السماء في الصلاة، لينتهن أو لتخطفن أبصارهم»(۱) وفي التجنيس : ويكره أن يميل أصابع يديه ورجليه عن القبلة لأنه مأمور بتوجيهها قال عليه السلام «فليوجه من أعضائه إلى القبلة ما استطاع». يؤدي في صلاته بموق عينيه ولعل المراد عند عدم الحاجة فلا ينافي ما هنا قوله : (وكأنه جمع الخ) قال في النهر: فيه بحث اهـ. وفي شرح نظم الكنز للعلامة المقدسي : لكن ظهر لي والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم أن مراد الخلاصة بتحويل الوجه المفسد تحويل جميعه عن القبلة وذلك يلزم منه تحويل الصدر لأن الوجه ليس بمستو بل فيه استدارة فإذا حول عن القبلة بأن أزيل بعضه عن مسامتتها كالجانب الأيمن منه بقي الجانب الأيسر منه مسامتاً فلا تفسد فإذا حول الجميع كان الصدر أيضاً محولاً فتفسد الصلاة ولهذا قالوا في باب استقبال القبلة : لا تفسد إلا بتحوله من المشارق إلى المغارب فليتأمل اهـ . قلت : ويشعر بذلك جعل الخانية الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه ولعل هذا مراد النهر بالبحث فيما قاله المؤلف قوله : ( ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية (الخ) كأنه لم ير فيه نقلاً صريحاً وقد رأيت في الحاوي
His saying (and turning away): because of what Bukhari narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) who said: I asked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) about turning away in prayer, and he said: 'It is a snatching that the devil snatches from the prayer of the servant.' And Tirmidhi narrated and authenticated from Anas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Beware of turning away in prayer, for turning away in prayer is destruction. If it must be, then in voluntary prayer, not in obligatory.' Then what is mentioned in most books is that disliked turning away is turning one's face away from the qiblah, and those who stated this include the author of al-Bada'i', al-Nihayah, al-Ghayah, al-Tabyin, Fath al-Qadir, al-Mujtaba, al-Kafi, and Sharh al-Majma'. Al-Ghayah qualified it as being without excuse; turning the face for an excuse is not disliked. It should be makruh tahrimi as is apparent from the hadith. They said: It is disliked without excuse because it is a deviation from the qiblah with part of his body. If he deviates with his entire body, his prayer becomes invalid. So if he deviates with part of his body, it is disliked, like a small amount of action, which is disliked because its abundance invalidates. The evidence that this turning does not invalidate is the Prophet's saying in the hadith 'the devil snatches it from the prayer of the servant' because he called it prayer while it occurs. And it is not disliked when there is an excuse due to the hadith of Muslim from Jabir: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) became ill, so we prayed behind him while he was sitting, and he turned to us and saw us standing, so he gestured to us to sit down.' And they stated that moving the eyes right and left without turning the face at all is not disliked at all, but it is better to leave it when there is no need. It seems that his action (peace be upon him) was due to the need to check the conditions of those following him, along with indicating permissibility. Otherwise, he used to see behind him as he saw in front of him as in the two Sahihs. And al-Saruji differed in his saying (and it is what is above the tafṭafah and sharāsīf): al-ṭafṭafah is the sides of the loins, and al-sharāsīf are the ends of the rib that overhangs the belly, according to al-Mughrib. His saying (and it is better to leave it without need) meaning it would be makruh tanzihi, as it is the reference for khilaf al-awla as previously mentioned, and al-Nahr stated this clearly. In al-Zayla'i and Sharh al-Multaqa by al-Baqqani, it is permissible because he used to observe his companions from his [?]. The Khulasah (summary) of the general books on disliked turning made it invalidating, stating: 'If the praying person turns his face away from the qiblah without excuse, his prayer becomes invalid.' Similarly in al-Khaniyyah, where disliked turning is defined as turning part of the face from the qiblah. The more appropriate view is what is in the general books: that disliked turning includes turning all or part of the face. It is mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli that the dislike of turning with the face applies when he faces [the qiblah] immediately. Meaning, if he does not face immediately, it becomes invalid. It is as if he combined between the fatwas and the general books by interpreting the fatwas to apply when he does not face immediately, and the general books to apply when he faces immediately. And it is as if he considered that if he does not face immediately, it becomes many actions and invalidates, and if he faces immediately, it is a small action and is disliked. But this is far-fetched, because continuity on this small action does not make it many; rather, its abundance is turning his chest. And they have stated that invalidity occurs when turning the chest. It must be qualified by absence of excuse, as in Munyat al-Musalli, due to their explicit statement as mentioned that if he thought he had broken wudu and turned his back to the qiblah, then learned he had not before leaving the mosque, it does not invalidate. And the requirements of the school's legal principles: the condition that he performs a pillar while facing away, because they stated that uncovering the private parts only invalidates if he does not cover immediately until he performs a pillar; but if he covers before performing the pillar, it does not invalidate. Similarly, facing the qiblah, considering the common factor of conditionality, and staying for the duration of performing a pillar, there is a difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Abu Yusuf does not consider it like performing a pillar, while Muhammad considers it as known. And the commentator stated that it is disliked to raise his eyes to the sky, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'What is wrong with people who raise their eyes to the sky during prayer? Let they desist, or their eyesight will be snatched away.' And in al-Tajnis: It is disliked to incline the fingers of his hands and feet from the qiblah because he is commanded to direct them. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Let him direct from his limbs to the qiblah as much as he can.' He performs in his prayer with the corner of his eyes; perhaps the meaning is when there is no need, so it does not contradict what is here. His saying (and it is as if he combined) etc. Al-Nahr said: there is discussion here. In Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by al-Maqdisi: But it appears to me, and Allah knows best, that the meaning of al-Khulasah's 'invalidating turning of the face' is turning all of it from the qiblah, and that necessitates turning the chest because the face is not straight but has curvature. So if he turns from the qiblah by removing part of it from alignment, like the right side, the left side remains aligned, so it does not invalidate. If he turns all of it, the chest also turns, so prayer becomes invalid. For this reason they said in the chapter on facing the qiblah: 'It only invalidates if he turns from the east to the west.' So let it be pondered. I say: This is indicated by al-Khaniyyah's making disliked turning as turning part of his face, and perhaps this is what al-Nahr meant by discussion in what the author said. His saying (and the requirements of the school's legal principles) etc. It is as if he did not see an explicit text, and I have seen in al-Hawi...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the ruling of turning away (iltifat) in prayer. The author begins by citing two hadiths: one from Bukhari where the Prophet (peace be upon him) describes iltifat as a snatching by the devil, and another from Tirmidhi warning against it and stating that if it must be done, it should be in nafl, not fard. The author then clarifies the definition of disliked iltifat: according to most Hanafi books, it is turning the face away from the qiblah without excuse. If there is an excuse, it is not disliked. The author opines that it should be makruh tahrimi based on the hadiths. The reason for dislike without excuse is that it is a deviation from the qiblah with part of the body; if the entire body deviates, the prayer is invalidated. To show that this partial turn does not nullify prayer, the author points to the hadith calling it 'snatching from prayer', implying it is still prayer. Excused turning is supported by a hadith from Muslim where the Prophet turned to see his companions while leading prayer due to illness. Moving the eyes without turning the face is not disliked at all, though better avoided. The author then discusses a difference between al-Khulasah and other books: some say turning part of the face invalidates; others say only turning all the face invalidates. He resolves by explaining that if one does not immediately face the qiblah again, it becomes many actions and invalidates; otherwise, it is just disliked. He also notes that turning the chest invalidates. The author then mentions the disliked acts of raising eyes to the sky and tilting fingers and feet from the qiblah, citing relevant hadiths. Finally, he discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about whether remaining facing away for the duration of a pillar counts as performing a pillar. The passage concludes with a remark from al-Maqdisi clarifying that turning the entire face necessarily turns the chest and thus invalidates, while turning only part of the face does not. This summary reflects the passage's focus on the details of iltifat and related disliked acts.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifāt): Turning the face away from the qiblah during prayer.
مكث قدر أداء الركن (mukth qadr adā' al-rukun): Remaining in a state (e.g., facing away) for the duration it takes to perform one pillar (e.g., a short recitation or bowing).
شرطية (sharṭiyyah): The quality of being a condition (shart) for the validity of the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Muhammad's view that remaining facing away for the duration of a pillar invalidates the prayer?
A) A direct command from the Prophet.
B) An analogy to the rule of covering the private parts.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) The principle that any deviation is nullifying.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether prolonged face-turning (without chest) invalidates prayer; the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view that it does not.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
7 min
Other Disliked Actions in Prayer: Raising Eyes, Tilting Fingers, and Eye Movement
Rulings on other disliked actions in prayer with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling on raising the eyes to the sky during prayer, tilting the fingers and feet from the qiblah, and moving the eyes without turning the face, based on Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Raising eyes to the sky and tilting limbs from the qiblah are disliked; moving eyes without head movement is permissible but better avoided.

Beyond turning the face, the Hanafi school identifies several other actions that are disliked in prayer. This lesson covers the relevant hadith proofs and the threshold for dislike for raising the eyes, tilting the fingers and feet, and moving the eyes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action: raising eyes to the sky
The commentator states that it is disliked to raise the eyes to the sky during prayer. The proof is the hadith warning that people who do so should desist or their eyesight will be snatched away.
This indicates a strong prohibition, hence the dislike.
2
Identify the action: tilting fingers and feet from qiblah
According to al-Tajnis, it is disliked to incline the fingers of the hands and feet away from the qiblah because the person is commanded to direct all limbs towards qiblah.
The hadith cited: 'Let him direct from his limbs to the qiblah as much as he can.'
3
Identify the action: moving eyes without turning face
The text states explicitly that moving the eyes right and left without turning the face is not disliked at all (ghayru makrūh mutlaqan). However, it is better to avoid it when there is no need.
This is the most lenient ruling among these actions.
4
Determine the hukm level for each
Raising eyes and tilting limbs: makruh tanzihi (or possibly makruh tahrimi for raising eyes given the strong warning). Moving eyes: mubah (permissible) but better avoided (khilaf al-awla).
The passage uses 'يكره' for raising eyes and tilting fingers, implying makruh. For eye movement, it says 'غير مكروه' and 'الأولى تركه'.
5
Note the exception for need
All these actions may be permissible if there is a genuine need. For example, the Prophet himself turned (face) to check on his companions. Similarly, raising eyes or tilting limbs for a valid reason may be excused.
The principle of need overrides the dislike.

قوله (والالتفات) لما رواه البخاري عن عائشة الله عنها قالت: سألت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الالتفات في الصلاة فقال : هو اختلاس يختلسه الشيطان من صلاة العبد. وروى الترمذي وصححه عن أنس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إياك والالتفات في الصلاة فإن الالتفات في الصلاة ،هلكة فإن كان لا بد ففي التطوع لا في الفريضة»(٢). ثم المذكور في عامة الكتب لأن الالتفات المكروه هو تحويل وجهه عن القبلة وممن صرح به صاحب البدائع والنهاية والغاية والتبيين وفتح القدير والمجتبي والكافي وشرح المجمع، وقيده في الغاية بأن يكون لغير عذر، أما تحويل الوجه لعذر فغير مكروه. وينبغي أن تكون تحريمية كما هو ظاهر الأحاديث قالوا : وإنما كره لغير عذر لأنه انحراف عن القبلة ببعض بدنه ولو انحرف عنها بجميع بدنه فسدت فإن انحرف ببعض بدنه كره كالعمل القليل فإنه مكروه لأن كثيره مفسد ويدل لعدم فسادها بهذا الالتفات قوله في الحديث يختلسها الشيطان من صلاة العبد فإنه سماها صلاة معه. وإنما لم يكره للعذر الحديث مسلم عن جابر اشتکی رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلينا وراءه وهو قاعد فالتفت إلينا فرآنا قياماً فأشار إلينا فقعدنا وقد صرحوا بأن التفات البصر يمنة ويسرة من غير تحويل الوجه أصلاً غير مكروه مطلقاً والأولى تركه لغير حاجة، والظاهر أن فعله عليه السلام إياه كان لحاجة تفقد أحوال المقتدين به مع ما فيه من بيان الجواز وإلا فهو كان ينظر من خلفه كما ينظر أمامه كما في الصحيحين. وقد خالف صاحب السروجي قوله : (وهي ما فوق الطفطفة والشراسيف) الطفطفة أطراف الخاصرة، والشراسيف أطراف الضلع الذي يشرف على البطن نهاية عن المغرب. قوله : ( والأولى تركه لغير حاجة أي فيكون مكروهاً تنزيهاً كما هو مرجع خلاف الأولى كما مر وبه صرح في النهر، وفي الزيلعي وشرح الملتقى للباقاني أنه مباح لأنه لما كان يلاحظ أصحابه من ساعته الخلاصة عامة الكتب في الالتفات المكروه فجعله مفسداً وعبارته: ولو حول المصلي وجهه القبلة من غير عذر فسدت وكذا في الخانية وجعل فيها الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه عن القبلة والأشبه ما في عامة الكتب من أن الالتفات المكروه أعم من Similarly in al-Khaniyyah, where disliked turning is defined as turning part of the face from the qiblah. The more appropriate view is what is in the general books: that disliked turning includes turning all or part of the face. It is mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli that the dislike of turning with the face applies when he faces [the qiblah] immediately. Meaning, if he does not face immediately, it becomes invalid. It is as if he combined between the fatwas and the general books by interpreting the fatwas to apply when he does not face immediately, and the general books to apply when he faces immediately. And it is as if he considered that if he does not face immediately, it becomes many actions and invalidates, and if he faces immediately, it is a small action and is disliked. But this is far-fetched, because continuity on this small action does not make it many; rather, its abundance is turning his chest. And they have stated that invalidity occurs when turning the chest. It must be qualified by absence of excuse, as in Munyat al-Musalli, due to their explicit statement as mentioned that if he thought he had broken wudu and turned his back to the qiblah, then learned he had not before leaving the mosque, it does not invalidate. And the requirements of the school's legal principles: the condition that he performs a pillar while facing away, because they stated that uncovering the private parts only invalidates if he does not cover immediately until he performs a pillar; but if he covers before performing the pillar, it does not invalidate. Similarly, facing the qiblah, considering the common factor of conditionality, and staying for the duration of performing a pillar, there is a difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Abu Yusuf does not consider it like performing a pillar, while Muhammad considers it as known. And the commentator stated that it is disliked to raise his eyes to the sky, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'What is wrong with people who raise their eyes to the sky during prayer? Let they desist, or their eyesight will be snatched away.' And in al-Tajnis: It is disliked to incline the fingers of his hands and feet from the qiblah because he is commanded to direct them. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Let him direct from his limbs to the qiblah as much as he can.' He performs in his prayer with the corner of his eyes; perhaps the meaning is when there is no need, so it does not contradict what is here. His saying (and it is as if he combined) etc. Al-Nahr said: there is discussion here. In Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by al-Maqdisi: But it appears to me, and Allah knows best, that the meaning of al-Khulasah's 'invalidating turning of the face' is turning all of it from the qiblah, and that necessitates turning the chest because the face is not straight but has curvature. So if he turns from the qiblah by removing part of it from alignment, like the right side, the left side remains aligned, so it does not invalidate. If he turns all of it, the chest also turns, so prayer becomes invalid. For this reason they said in the chapter on facing the qiblah: 'It only invalidates if he turns from the east to the west.' So let it be pondered. I say: This is indicated by al-Khaniyyah's making disliked turning as turning part of his face, and perhaps this is what al-Nahr meant by discussion in what the author said. His saying (and the requirements of the school's legal principles) etc. It is as if he did not see an explicit text, and I have seen in al-Hawi...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the ruling of turning away (iltifat) in prayer. The author begins by citing two hadiths: one from Bukhari where the Prophet (peace be upon him) describes iltifat as a snatching by the devil, and another from Tirmidhi warning against it and stating that if it must be done, it should be in nafl, not fard. The author then clarifies the definition of disliked iltifat: according to most Hanafi books, it is turning the face away from the qiblah without excuse. If there is an excuse, it is not disliked. The author opines that it should be makruh tahrimi based on the hadiths. The reason for dislike without excuse is that it is a deviation from the qiblah with part of the body; if the entire body deviates, the prayer is invalidated. To show that this partial turn does not nullify prayer, the author points to the hadith calling it 'snatching from prayer', implying it is still prayer. Excused turning is supported by a hadith from Muslim where the Prophet turned to see his companions while leading prayer due to illness. Moving the eyes without turning the face is not disliked at all, though better avoided. The author then discusses a difference between al-Khulasah and other books: some say turning part of the face invalidates; others say only turning all the face invalidates. He resolves by explaining that if one does not immediately face the qiblah again, it becomes many actions and invalidates; otherwise, it is just disliked. He also notes that turning the chest invalidates. The author then mentions the disliked acts of raising eyes to the sky and tilting fingers and feet from the qiblah, citing relevant hadiths. Finally, he discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about whether remaining facing away for the duration of a pillar counts as performing a pillar. The passage concludes with a remark from al-Maqdisi clarifying that turning the entire face necessarily turns the chest and thus invalidates, while turning only part of the face does not. This summary reflects the passage's focus on the details of iltifat and related disliked acts.

Key Terms
رفع البصر (raf' al-basar): Lifting the eyes to look upward (towards the sky) during prayer.
إمالة الأصابع (imālat al-aṣābi‘): Inclining the fingers of the hands or feet away from the direction of the qiblah.
التفات البصر (iltifāt al-basar): Moving the eyes to look to the right or left without turning the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on moving the eyes right and left without turning the head during prayer?
A) It is haram and invalidates the prayer.
B) It is makruh tahrimi.
C) It is not disliked at all (ghayru makrūh).
D) It is recommended (sunnah).

Key Takeaway
Raising eyes and tilting limbs from qiblah are disliked; moving eyes without head movement is not disliked but better avoided.

Ruling on Turning Face from Qibla in Prayer
Explains ruling on turning face from qibla and intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on turning the face from the qibla during prayer and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on when it invalidates.

Core Idea
Turning face from qibla invalidates prayer if not returned immediately; duration of a pillar is key.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the Hanafi ruling on turning the face away from the qibla during prayer. It explores the majority view that such turning is invalidating if not rectified immediately, and highlights the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the effect of pausing for the duration of a pillar. The discussion also touches on related issues like raising eyes to the sky and inclining fingers, emphasizing the importance of maintaining qibla orientation throughout prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What does the passage say about turning the face? Is it invalidating or disliked? Note the majority view and the view of al-Khaniyah.
The passage cites multiple sources: al-Khulasah and general books.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi differences
How do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the effect of pausing for a pillar's duration while not facing qibla?
Muhammad considers it invalidating; Abu Yusuf does not.
3
Apply the principle of 'amal kathir vs 'amal qalil
How does the duration of the turn affect the ruling? What makes an action 'excessive'?
The passage discusses that continuity on a small action does not make it large; turning the chest is the threshold.
4
Consider the condition of return (immediate vs prolonged)
What is the consensus view on immediate return? How does it affect the ruling?
If returned immediately, it is disliked; if prolonged (for a pillar's duration), it invalidates.
5
Recognize the exception for valid excuses
What happens if the worshipper turns due to a mistaken belief (e.g., thinking wudu broke)?
The prayer is not invalidated if returned upon realizing the mistake.

ساعته
الخلاصة عامة الكتب في الالتفات المكروه فجعله مفسداً وعبارته: ولو حول المصلي وجهه القبلة من غير عذر فسدت وكذا في الخانية وجعل فيها الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه عن القبلة والأشبه ما في عامة الكتب من أن الالتفات المكروه أعم من تحويل جميع الوجه أو بعضه. وذكر في منية المصلي أن كراهة الالتفات بالوجه فيما إذا استقبل من . يعني فلو لم يستقبل من ساعته فسدت وكأنه جمع بين ما في الفتاوى وبين ما في عامة الكتب بحمل ما في الفتاوى على ما إذا لم يستقبل من ساعته وحمل ما في العامة على ما إذا استقبل ساعته وكأنه ناظر إلى أنه إذا لم يستقبل من ساعته صار عملاً كثيراً فأفسدها، وإذا استقبل ساعته كان عملاً قليلاً فكره وهو بعيد فإن الاستدامة على هذا القليل لا يجعله كثيراً وإنما كثيره تحويل صدره، وقد صرحوا بالفساد عند تحويل الصدر. ولا بد من تقييده بعدم العذر كما في منية المصلي لتصريحهم كما سبق بأنه لو ظن أنه أحدث فاستدبر القبلة ثم علم أنه لم يحدث قبل الخروج من المسجد لا تبطل، ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية اشتراط أن ركناً وهو مستدبر لما صرحوا به من أن انكشاف العورة إنما يفسدها إذا لم يستتر من ساعته حتى أدى ركناً أما إذا سترها قبل أداء الركن فلا ، فكذا استقبال القبلة بجامع الشرطية والمكث قدر أداء الركن فيه خلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد؛ فأبو يوسف لا يجعله كأداء الركن، ومحمد جعله كما عرف. وذكر الشارح أنه يكره رفع بصره إلى السماء لقوله عليه السلام «ما بال أقوام يرفعون أبصارهم إلى السماء في الصلاة، لينتهن أو لتخطفن أبصارهم»(۱) وفي التجنيس : ويكره أن يميل أصابع يديه ورجليه عن القبلة لأنه مأمور بتوجيهها قال عليه السلام «فليوجه من أعضائه إلى القبلة ما استطاع».

في صلاته بموق عينيه ولعل المراد عند عدم الحاجة فلا ينافي ما هنا قوله : (وكأنه جمع الخ) قال في النهر : فيه بحث اهـ. وفي شرح نظم الكنز للعلامة المقدسي : لكن ظهر لي والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم أن مراد الخلاصة بتحويل الوجه المفسد تحويل جميعه عن القبلة وذلك يلزم منه تحويل الصدر لأن الوجه ليس بمستو بل فيه استدارة فإذا حول عن القبلة بأن أزيل بعضه عن مسامتتها كالجانب الأيمن منه بقي الجانب الأيسر منه مسامتاً فلا تفسد فإذا حول الجميع كان الصدر أيضاً محولاً فتفسد الصلاة ولهذا قالوا في باب استقبال القبلة : لا تفسد إلا بتحوله من المشارق إلى المغارب فليتأمل اهـ . قلت : ويشعر بذلك جعل الخانية الالتفات المكروه أن يحول بعض وجهه ولعل هذا مراد النهر بالبحث فيما قاله المؤلف قوله : ( ومقتضى القواعد المذهبية (الخ) كأنه لم ير فيه نقلاً صريحاً وقد رأيت في الحاوي
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الأذان باب ۹۲ مسلم في كتاب الصلاة حديث ۱۱۸. أبو داود في كتاب الصلاة باب ١٦٣ . النسائي في كتاب السهو باب ۹ ابن ماجه في كتاب الإقامة باب ٦٨. أحمد في مسنده (۲/ ۲۲۳) (۳/ ۱۰۹، ۱۱۲).
The Khulasah, and generally the books, regarding disliked turning: they made it invalidating. Its expression: 'If the praying person turns his face from the qibla without an excuse, his prayer is invalidated.' Likewise in al-Khaniyah, and he made in it the disliked turning that he turns part of his face from the qibla. And the more appropriate is what is in generally the books: that disliked turning is more general than turning all of the face or part of it. And it is mentioned in Munyat al-Musalli that the dislike of turning with the face is if he faced [the qibla] immediately? Meaning: if he did not face immediately, it is invalidated. It seems he reconciled between what is in al-Fatawa and what is in the general books by interpreting what is in al-Fatawa to apply if he does not face immediately, and interpreting what is in the general books to apply if he faces immediately. It seems he considers that if he does not face immediately, it becomes a lot of action, thus invalidates; if he faces immediately, it is a little action, thus disliked. But this is far-fetched, because continuity on this little action does not make it a lot; rather, its excess is turning his chest. They explicitly stated invalidation upon turning the chest. It must be qualified with lack of excuse, as in Munyat al-Musalli, because they explicitly stated, as previously, that if he thought he had passed wind and turned his back to the qibla, then found he had not passed wind before leaving the mosque, it is not invalidated. The implications of the school's principles require that he performs a pillar while turning back, due to their explicit statement that uncovering the private part only invalidates if he does not cover immediately until he performs a pillar; if he covers before performing the pillar, it does not invalidate. Similarly for facing the qibla, by the common ground of conditionality. And pausing for the duration of performing a pillar: there is a difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf does not consider it like performing a pillar, while Muhammad considers it as known. And the commentator mentioned that it is disliked to raise one's sight to the sky due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'What is wrong with people who raise their eyes to the sky during prayer? They must desist, or their eyesight will be taken away.' In al-Tajnis: It is disliked to incline the fingers of the hands and feet away from the qibla, because he is commanded to direct them. He said, peace be upon him: 'Let him direct from his limbs towards the qibla as much as he can.' ... [remaining translation abbreviated due to length]
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on turning the face away from the qibla during prayer. The author, al-Marghinani (or the commentator of Kanz al-Daqa'iq), presents a discussion from several sources. The core issue: is turning the face from the qibla an invalidator (mufsid) or merely disliked (makruh)? According to al-Khulasah and most books, if a worshipper turns his face from the qibla without an excuse, the prayer is invalidated. However, al-Khaniyah distinguishes between turning the whole face and turning part of it, considering only the latter as disliked. The author leans toward the majority view that any turning (whole or part) is disliked unless the person does not return immediately; if he stays turned for a duration equal to performing a pillar, it becomes invalidating due to excessive action. He notes that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether pausing for the duration of a pillar while not facing qibla is equivalent to actually performing a pillar. Muhammad considers it invalidating, Abu Yusuf does not. The commentator also adds that raising eyes to the sky is disliked, citing the hadith threatening loss of eyesight. Inclining fingers and feet from the qibla is also disliked. The passage ends by examining the meaning of 'turning the face' in al-Khulasah, suggesting it means turning the entire face, which necessarily involves turning the chest, thus invalidating. The author cautions that this discussion assumes no excuse; with an excuse (e.g. thinking one's wudu broke), the prayer is not invalidated even if one turns away, provided one returns upon realizing the mistake.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the face away from the qibla during prayer.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike; in this context, likely karahah tahrimiyyah due to prohibition hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when does turning the face from the qibla invalidate the prayer?
A) Whenever the worshipper turns his face even slightly.
B) Only if he turns his chest completely.
C) If he does not return his face to the qibla immediately and remains turned for the duration of performing a pillar.
D) It never invalidates; it is only disliked.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, turning the face from qibla invalidates prayer if not returned immediately; the duration of a pillar is the threshold for excessive action.

Ruling on Iqa' (Sitting on Heels) in Prayer
Explains the prohibition of iqa' and the intra-Hanafi definitional dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqa' in prayer and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over its definition, distinguishing between the two types.

Core Idea
All forms of iqa' in prayer are makruh tahrimi — prohibited, not recommended.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Rai'q clarifies the ruling on iqa' (sitting on heels) during prayer. While the hadiths clearly prohibit certain postures, scholars within the Hanafi school have debated the exact definition of the prohibited iqa'. This lesson will guide you through the evidence, the two main definitions, and the final consensus that both forms are makruh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith prohibition
The passage cites the hadith forbidding iqa' like a dog and the 'sitting of the devil'. Note that this prohibition indicates makruh tahrimi.
The hadith is from Muslim and Ahmad.
2
Contrast the two Hanafi definitions
The majority (al-Hidayah, al-Tahawi) define iqa' as sitting on buttocks with knees upright, often with hands on ground. Al-Karkhi defines it as sitting on heels with hands on ground.
Both definitions are discussed in the source.
3
Understand the ruling applies to both
The text stresses that both types are makruh, as they abandon the sunnah sitting. Fath al-Qadir confirms that even if one definition is primary, the other is still disliked.
This unifies the practical ruling.
4
Address the apparent contradiction
The passage addresses narrations from Ibn 'Abbas and others that seem to endorse iqa'. The Hanafi response distinguishes between a recommended type (buttocks on heels) and the prohibited type (hands on ground). The author emphasizes that this distinction is not from Hanafi imams; in our school, both are makruh.
This shows the Hanafi commitment to the hadith prohibition.

قوله (والإقعاء) لنهيه الا الله عن عقبة الشيطان كما في الصحيحين وهو الإقعاء ولما في مسند أحمد عن أبي هريرة: نهاني رسول الله عن ثلاثة : عن نقرة كنقرة الديك وإقعاء كإقعاء الكلب والتفات كالتفات الثعلب شبه من يسرع في الركوع والسجود ويخفف فيهما بالديك الذي يلتقط الحبة كما في النهاية وهي كراهة تحريم للنهي المذكور كما أسلفناه من الأصل. ثم اختلفوا في الإقعاء المذكور في الحديث. فصحح صاحب الهداية وعامتهم أنه أن أليتيه على الأرض وينصب ركبتيه نصباً كما هو قول الطحاوي. وزاد كثير ويضع يديه على الأرض، وزاد بعضهم أن يضم ركبتيه إلى صدره لأن إقعاء الكلب يكون بهذه الصفة إلا أن إقعاء الكلب يكون في نصب اليدين وإقعاء الآدمي في نصب الركبتين إلى صدره. وذهب الكرخي إلى أنه أن ينصب قدميه ويقعد على عقبيه واضعاً يديه على الأرض وهو عقب الشيطان الذي نهى عنه في الحديث والكل مكروه لأن فيه ترك الجلسة المسنونة. كذا . البدائع وغاية البيان والمجتبي . زاد في فتح القدير أن قوله الصحيح أي كون هذا هو المراد في الحديث لا أن ما قاله الكرخي غير مكروه بل يكره ذلك أيضاً اهـ. والعُقبة بضم العين وسكون القاف، والعَقِب بفتح العين وكسر القاف بمعنى الإقعاء. كذا في المغرب. وفي فتح القدير : وأما ما روى مسلم عن طاوس قلت لابن عباس في الإقعاء على القدمين فقال : السنة. فقلت : إنا نراه جفاء بالرجل فقال : بل هي سنة نبيك . . وما روى البيهقي عن ابن عمر وابن الزبير أنهم كانوا يقعون فالجواب المحقق عنه أن الإقعاء على ضربين: أحدهما مستحب أن يضع أليتيه على عقبيه وركبتاه في الأرض وهو المروي عن العبادلة، والنهي أن يضع أليتيه ويديه على الأرض وينصب ساقييه اهـ. وهو مخالف لما ذكره هو وغيره أن الإقعاء بنوعيه مكروه، والحق أن هذا الجواب ليس لأئمتنا وإنما هو جواب البيهقي والنووي وغيرهما بناء على أن مستحب عند الشافعي لأنك قد علمت كراهته عندنا بنوعيه. ويمكن الجواب عنه
هي
القدسي ما ظاهره ذلك حيث قال في مفسدات الصلاة وكذا استدبار القبلة وانكشاف العورة مقدار أداء ركن من غير عذر قوله : ( وهو عقب الشيطان الخ) أي الإقعاء على التفسير الثاني الذي قاله الكرخي هو المراد بعقب الشيطان المنهي عنه في الحديث الآخر، وهذا موافق لما سيأتي عن المغرب لكن نقل العلامة قاسم في فتاواه عن لسان العرب والنهاية لابن الأثير أن عقبة الشيطان أن يجلس على قدميه بين السجدتين اهـ. مع أن الإقعاء مكروه في التشهدين أيضاً. قال العلامة قاسم من غير خلاف نعلمه بين أصحاب المذاهب نص على كراهته من علمائنا الكرخي في المختصر اهـ فليتأمل. قوله : (والحق أن هذا الجواب ليس لأئمتنا الخ يؤيده ما قاله العلامة قاسم في فتاواه وأما نصب القدمين والجلوس على العقبين فمكروه في جميع الجلسات من غير خلاف نعرفه إلا ما ذكره الشيخ الدين النووي عن الشافعي في قول له إنه يستحب الجلوس بين السجدتين بهذه الصفة. قال محمد رحمه الله في
حي
His statement (and iqa‘) due to his prohibition, peace be upon him, from the sitting of the devil as in the Two Sahihs, and that is iqa‘. And because in the Musnad of Ahmad from Abu Hurairah: ‘The Messenger of Allah forbade me from three things: from pecking like a rooster, from iqa‘ like a dog, and from turning like a fox.’ He compared the one who hurries in bowing and prostrating and lightens them to a rooster that pecks a grain, as in al-Nihayah. It is makruh tahrimi due to the mentioned prohibition, as we have established from the principle. Then they differed over iqa‘ mentioned in the hadith. The author of al-Hidayah and the majority of them considered it to be that one places his buttocks on the ground and keeps his knees upright, as is the view of al-Tahawi. And many added: and places his hands on the ground. Some added: and pulls his knees to his chest, because the iqa‘ of a dog is of this description, except that the iqa‘ of a dog involves raising the hands, while the iqa‘ of a human involves raising the knees to the chest. And al-Karkhi held that it is to stand on the feet and sit on the heels, placing the hands on the ground, and that is the ‘sitting of the devil’ which is prohibited in the hadith. All are makruh because they involve abandoning the sunnah sitting. Kama fi al-Bada’i‘, Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Mujtaba. In Fath al-Qadir: his saying ‘the sound’ means that this is the intended meaning in the hadith, not that what al-Karkhi said is not makruh; rather that too is makruh. (End). And ‘uqbah (with damma on ‘ayn and sukun on qaf) and ‘aqib (with fatha on ‘ayn and kasra on qaf) mean iqa‘. Kama fi al-Mughrib. In Fath al-Qadir: As for what Muslim narrated from Ta’us: ‘I said to Ibn ‘Abbas about iqa‘ on the feet, and he said: It is the sunnah. I said: We see it as roughness to the man. He said: Rather it is the sunnah of your Prophet...’ And what al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ibn ‘Umar and Ibn al-Zubayr that they used to do iqa‘? The verified answer about it is that iqa‘ is of two types: one is recommended: to place the buttocks on the heels and the knees on the ground, and that is narrated from the ‘Abadilah. The prohibited one is to place the buttocks and hands on the ground and raise the shins. This differs from what he and others mentioned: that both types of iqa‘ are makruh. The truth is that this answer is not from our imams; rather it is the answer of al-Bayhaqi and al-Nawawi and others, based on the fact that it is recommended according to al-Shafi‘i, because you have known that both types are makruh in our school. And it is possible to answer it... [remaining translation omitted due to length]
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on iqa‘ (a specific sitting posture) in prayer. The author begins by stating the prohibition from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the 'sitting of the devil' and the hadith of Abu Hurairah forbidding pecking like a rooster, iqa‘ like a dog, and turning like a fox. This indicates that iqa‘ is makruh tahrimi. The scholars then differ on the exact definition of iqa‘. The majority (including the author of al-Hidayah and al-Tahawi) define it as sitting with buttocks on the ground and knees upright. Some add placing hands on the ground, while others add pulling knees to the chest, comparing to a dog. Al-Karkhi defines it differently: standing on the feet and sitting on the heels with hands on the ground, and he identifies this as the 'sitting of the devil' prohibited in the hadith. The author notes that all these forms are makruh because they abandon the sunnah sitting. In Fath al-Qadir, it is clarified that even if al-Karkhi's interpretation is not the sole meaning of the hadith, his form is still makruh. The passage then addresses a narration from Ta’us and Ibn ‘Abbas suggesting that sitting on the heels is sunnah, and reports from Ibn ‘Umar and Ibn al-Zubayr. The Hanafi response is that the recommended iqa‘ (placing buttocks on heels) is different from the prohibited one (placing buttocks and hands on ground). The author emphasizes that this distinction is from non-Hanafi scholars like al-Bayhaqi and al-Nawawi; in the Hanafi school, both types are makruh. The passage concludes by citing ‘Allamah Qasim in his Fatawa that there is no known disagreement among Hanafis that sitting on heels is makruh in all sittings, contrary to the Shafi‘i view that it is recommended between prostrations.

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iqa'): A sitting posture in prayer involving sitting on the buttocks with knees raised, or sitting on the heels with hands on ground.
عقبة الشيطان (uqbat al-shaytan): A specific sitting posture prohibited in hadith, identified by al-Karkhi as sitting on heels with hands on ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on iqa' (sitting on heels) in prayer?
A) It is recommended (mustahabb) between prostrations.
B) It is makruh tanzihi and can be done occasionally.
C) It is makruh tahrimi, prohibited in all sittings.
D) It is permissible as long as it does not delay the prayer.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, all forms of iqa' (sitting on heels with hands on ground or knees raised) are makruh tahrimi in all sittings of prayer.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Disliked Postures in Prayer: Iq'a' and Iftirash al-Dhira'ayn
Commentary on rulings of iq'a' and iftirash

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on iq'a' and iftirash al-dhira'ayn, distinguish between the two definitions of iq'a', and explain the difference in karahah levels (tanzihi vs. tahrimi).

Core Idea
Sitting like a dog or spreading arms like a beast in prayer is disliked—but the severity depends on which type of iq'a' you practice.

Al-Marghinani expands on two disliked acts in prayer from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: al-iq'a' and iftirash al-dhira'ayn. While both are makruh, a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement affects the degree of dislike for iq'a'. This lesson unpacks the proofs, definitions, and ruling differences that a Hanafi student must know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their textual proofs
Locate the hadiths cited for each act: the prohibition of 'uqbat al-shaytan and the hadith of Abu Hurairah for iq'a'; the hadith of A'ishah for iftirash. Note that the proofs are ahad hadiths.
The dalil_type is Sunnah_ahad; thubut and dalala are zanni.
2
Distinguish the two definitions of iq'a'
Understand the majority definition (buttocks on ground, knees erect) versus al-Karkhi's definition (sitting on heels, feet erect). Recognize that both are called iq'a' in the sources.
The majority view is from al-Hidayah; al-Karkhi's definition links to the 'heel of Satan'.
3
Apply the ruling of karahah
Determine the reasoning: both leave the sunnah sitting. The majority's type is makruh tanzihi (only leaving sunnah); al-Karkhi's type is makruh tahrimi (explicit prohibition + leaving sunnah).
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the reversal of rulings if definitions are swapped.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction with the Shafi'i proof
Analyze the response to Ibn Abbas' hadith. Hanafis either categorize it as a different type of iq'a' (mustahabb outside prayer) or restrict it to outside prayer, maintaining that all iq'a' in prayer is makruh.
The reconciliation is based on distinguishing between two forms, not on challenging authenticity.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
State the operative ruling: both types of iq'a' are makruh in all sittings; the sitting on heels (al-Karkhi) is more severe (tahrimi). Iftirash is makruh tanzihi due to resemblance to animals.
The tarjih authority is from al-Hidayah and later fatawa works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (والإقعاء) لنهيه عليه السلام عن عقبة الشيطان كما في الصحيحين وهو الإقعاء ولما في مسند أحمد عن أبي هريرة: نهاني رسول الله عن ثلاثة : عن نقرة كنقرة الديك وإقعاء كإقعاء الكلب والتفات كالتفات الثعلب شبه من يسرع في الركوع والسجود ويخفف فيهما بالديك الذي يلتقط الحبة كما في النهاية وهي كراهة تحريم للنهي المذكور كما أسلفناه من الأصل. ثم اختلفوا في الإقعاء المذكور في الحديث. فصحح صاحب الهداية وعامتهم أنه أن يضع أليتيه على الأرض وينصب ركبتيه نصباً كما هو قول الطحاوي. وزاد كثير ويضع يديه على الأرض، وزاد بعضهم أن يضم ركبتيه إلى صدره لأن إقعاء الكلب يكون بهذه الصفة إلا أن إقعاء الكلب يكون في نصب اليدين وإقعاء الآدمي في نصب الركبتين إلى صدره. وذهب الكرخي إلى أنه أن ينصب قدميه ويقعد على عقبيه واضعاً يديه على الأرض وهو عقب الشيطان الذي نهى عنه في الحديث والكل مكروه لأن فيه ترك الجلسة المسنونة. كذا في البدائع وغاية البيان والمجتبي. زاد في فتح القدير أن قوله الصحيح أي كون هذا هو المراد في الحديث لا أن ما قاله الكرخي غير مكروه بل يكره ذلك أيضاً. والعقبة بضم العين وسكون القاف، والعقب بفتح العين وكسر القاف بمعنى الإقعاء. كذا في المغرب. وفي فتح القدير: وأما ما روى مسلم عن طاوس قلت لابن عباس في الإقعاء على القدمين فقال: السنة. فقلت: إنا نراه جفاء بالرجل فقال: بل هي سنة نبيك. وما روى البيهقي عن ابن عمر وابن الزبير أنهم كانوا يقعون فالجواب المحقق عنه أن الإقعاء على ضربين: أحدهما مستحب أن يضع أليتيه على عقبيه وركبتاه في الأرض وهو المروي عن العبادلة، والنهي أن يضع أليتيه ويديه على الأرض وينصب ساقيه. وهو مخالف لما ذكره هو وغيره أن الإقعاء بنوعيه مكروه، والحق أن هذا الجواب ليس لأئمتنا وإنما هو جواب البيهقي والنووي وغيرهما بناء على أن مستحب عند الشافعي لأنك قد علمت كراهته عندنا بنوعيه. ويمكن الجواب عنه بما في الحديث القدسي ما ظاهره ذلك حيث قال في مفسدات الصلاة وكذا استدبار القبلة وانكشاف العورة مقدار أداء ركن من غير عذر. قوله: (وهو عقب الشيطان الخ) أي الإقعاء على التفسير الثاني الذي قاله الكرخي هو المراد بعقب الشيطان المنهي عنه في الحديث الآخر، وهذا موافق لما سيأتي عن المغرب لكن نقل العلامة قاسم في فتاواه عن لسان العرب والنهاية لابن الأثير أن عقبة الشيطان أن يجلس على قدميه بين السجدتين. مع أن الإقعاء مكروه في التشهدين أيضاً. قال العلامة قاسم من غير خلاف نعلمه بين أصحاب المذاهب نص على كراهته من علمائنا الكرخي في المختصر. فليتأمل. قوله: (والحق أن هذا الجواب ليس لأئمتنا الخ) يؤيده ما قاله العلامة قاسم في فتاواه وأما نصب القدمين والجلوس على العقبين فمكروه في جميع الجلسات من غير خلاف نعرفه إلا ما ذكره الشيخ الدين النووي عن الشافعي في قول له إنه يستحب الجلوس بين السجدتين بهذه الصفة. قال محمد رحمه الله في كتاب الآثار: لا ينبغي أن يجلس على عقبيه بين السجدتين ولكنه يجلس بينهما كجلوسه في صلاته وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، وذكره الطحاوي عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهم الله. قوله: (أما بحمله على حالة العذر) ينافيه قوله بل هي سنة نبيك، وكذا قال العلامة المقدسي وحمله على حالة العذر بعيد لقوله هو سنة نبيك فليتأمل. قوله: (أو بحمله على كونه خارج الصلاة جزم به الشيخ إبراهيم الحلبي في شرحه على المنية حيث قال بعد نقله كلام الفتح: وهو محمول على خارج الصلاة فإن ما ذكر من الحديثين ليس فيه ما يدل على أن المراد القعود في الصلاة وإلا فوضع الأليتين على العقبين في الصلاة مكروه أيضاً لمخالفة الجلوس المسنون وهو افتراش الرجل اليسرى، ولكن يفهم حينئذ أن الإقعاء بنصب الركبتين مكروه خارج الصلاة أيضاً ولا بعد فيه لأنه جلوس الجفاة بخلاف الإحتباء إذ ليس فيه كراهة خارج الصلاة، والفرق بين الإحتباء والإقعاء أن الإحتباء يكون بشد الركبتين إلى الظهر عند نصبهما بيديه أو بثوب أو غيره وهو أكثر جلوس أشراف العرب. قوله: فكان مانعاً أي فيترجح على ما رواه مسلم والبيهقي مما يفيد إباحته ولكن لا يخفى عليك أن كون المراد من الإقعاء هو الإقعاء على ما ذهب إليه مخالفاً لما مر من أن الصحيح أن المراد به الإقعاء بالمعنى الأول فلم يكن المراد من الإقعاء في حديث أبي هريرة هو المراد من حديث عقب الشيطان فلا تعارض حينئذ فلا ترجيح. قلت: ولو أسقط قوله "وقد فسر صاحب المغرب الخ" لاستقام الجواب من غير إيهام لأن المراد بالمبيح ما مر عن مسلم والبيهقي وبالمانع حديث النهي عن عقب الشيطان فيكون مرجحاً على المبيح من غير توقف على أن يكون المراد من عقب الشيطان هو الإقعاء عند الكرخي فتدبر. قوله: (وينبغي الخ) قال في النهر: وإنما كانت تنزيهية على الثاني بناء على أن هذا الفعل ليس بإقعاء وإنما الكراهة لترك الجلسة المسنونة كما علل به في البدائع، ولو فسر الإقعاء بقول الكرخي تعاكست الأحكام. قلت: لا يخفى عليك ما في هذا الكلام لأن كلا من الفعلين يسمى إقعاء، وإنما الكلام في المراد في الحديث منهما كما مر فكان الصواب أن يقال: إنما كانت تنزيهية على الثاني بناء على أن هذا الفعل ليس بمراد في الحديث أي فلا يكون داخلاً تحت النهي وإنما كره لترك الجلسة المسنونة فتكون فيه تحريمية لوجود النهي وترك الجلسة المسنونة، ولو أريد بالإقعاء في الحديث تنزيهية بخلاف النوع الأول فهي فـ الإقعاء عند الكرخي كان هو المكروه تحريماً لوجود الأمرين السابقين وكان الأول مكروها تنزيهاً لعدم النهي وبعد هذا فيه بحث أيضاً لأن عقب الشيطان هو الإقعاء تفسير الكرخي كما ذكره المؤلف عن المغرب فقد وجد. قوله وافتراش ذراعيه) لما في صحيح مسلم عن عائشة رضي الله عنها: وكان - يعني النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم - ينهى أن يفترش الرجل ذراعيه ذلك افتراش السبع. وافتراشهما إلقاؤهما على الأرض كما في المغرب. قيل: وإنما نهى عنها لأنها صفة الكسلان والتهاون بحاله مع ما فيه من التشبه بالسباع والكلاب.
His statement (and al-iq'a'): because of the prohibition of the Prophet, peace be upon him, from the 'heel of Satan' as in the two Sahihs, and that is iq'a'. And as in Musnad Ahmad from Abu Hurairah: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade me from three things: from pecking like a rooster, from iq'a' like a dog, and from turning like a fox.' He likens one who rushes in ruku' and sujud to a rooster pecking a grain, as in al-Nihayah. It is a makruh tahrimi because of the mentioned prohibition as we have established from the principle. Then they differed regarding iq'a' mentioned in the hadith. The author of al-Hidayah and their majority corrected that it is to place one's buttocks on the ground and erect his knees erectly, as is the saying of al-Tahawi. And many added: and he places his hands on the ground. And some added: and he draws his knees to his chest because the iq'a' of the dog is in this manner except that the iq'a' of the dog is in erecting the hands and the iq'a' of the human is in erecting the knees to his chest. And al-Karkhi held that it is to erect his feet and sit on his heels, placing his hands on the ground, and that is the 'heel of Satan' which is prohibited in the hadith. And all of it is makruh because it involves leaving the sunnah sitting. Thus in al-Bada'i', Ghayat al-Bayan, and al-Mujtaba. In Fath al-Qadir it is added: his saying 'the correct' means that this is what is meant in the hadith, not that what al-Karkhi said is not makruh; rather, that is also makruh. And al-'uqbah (with damm on 'ayn and sukun on qaf) and al-'aqib (with fatha on 'ayn and kasra on qaf) mean iq'a'. Thus in al-Mughrib. And in Fath al-Qadir: As for what Muslim narrated from Tawus: I said to Ibn Abbas regarding iq'a' on the feet, he said: 'It is the sunnah.' I said: 'We consider it coarse to the man.' He said: 'Rather, it is the sunnah of your Prophet.' And what al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ibn Umar and Ibn al-Zubayr that they used to do iq'a'. The verified answer about it is that iq'a' is of two types: one is mustahabb: to place his buttocks on his heels and his knees on the ground, and that is narrated from the 'Abadilah; and the prohibition is to place his buttocks and hands on the ground and erect his legs. And this contradicts what he and others mentioned that iq'a' of both types is makruh. The truth is that this answer is not from our imams; rather, it is the answer of al-Bayhaqi and al-Nawawi and others based on it being mustahabb according to al-Shafi'i, because you have learned that it is makruh according to us in both types. And it is possible to answer it with what is in the hadith qudsi whose apparent meaning is that, where he said in the invalidators of prayer: and likewise facing away from the qiblah and uncovering the 'awrah to the extent of performing a rukn without excuse. His statement: (and that is the 'heel of Satan' etc.) means: iq'a' according to the second interpretation that al-Karkhi said is what is meant by the 'heel of Satan' prohibited in the other hadith, and this agrees with what will come from al-Mughrib, but 'Allamah Qasim in his Fatawa quoted from Lisan al-'Arab and al-Nihayah of Ibn al-Athir that the 'heel of Satan' is to sit on the feet between the two sajdahs. Even though iq'a' is makruh in the two tashahhuds as well. 'Allamah Qasim said: without any disagreement we know among the followers of the schools, a text on its dislike from our scholars al-Karkhi in al-Mukhtasar. So let it be considered. His statement: (and the truth is that this answer is not from our imams etc.) is supported by what 'Allamah Qasim said in his Fatawa: and as for erecting the feet and sitting on the heels, it is makruh in all sittings without any disagreement we know, except what Shaykh al-Din al-Nawawi mentioned from al-Shafi'i in one of his sayings that it is mustahabb to sit between the two sajdahs in this manner. Muhammad (rahimahullah) said in Kitab al-Athar: 'He should not sit on his heels between the two sajdahs, but he should sit between them like his sitting in his prayer,' and that is the saying of Abu Hanifah (rahimahullah), and al-Tahawi mentioned it from Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (rahimahumullah). His statement: (either by carrying it to the case of excuse) contradicts his saying 'Rather, it is the sunnah of your Prophet.' Likewise, 'Allamah al-Maqdisi said: carrying it to the case of excuse is far because he said 'it is the sunnah of your Prophet.' So let it be considered. His statement: (or by carrying it to being outside the prayer) was affirmed by Shaykh Ibrahim al-Halabi in his commentary on al-Munyah where he said after quoting the words of al-Fath: 'It is carried to being outside the prayer, because what is mentioned in the two hadiths does not indicate that what is meant is sitting in the prayer; otherwise, placing the buttocks on the heels in the prayer is also makruh due to opposing the sunnah sitting, which is spreading the left leg. But it is understood then that iq'a' by erecting the knees is makruh outside the prayer as well, and there is no problem in it because it is the sitting of the coarse, unlike al-ihitiba' which has no dislike outside the prayer, and the difference between ihtiba' and iq'a' is that ihtiba' involves tying the knees to the back when erecting them with the hands or a cloth or other, and it is the most common sitting of the Arab nobles.' His statement: 'so it was a preventive' means it outweighs what Muslim and al-Bayhaqi narrated that indicates its permissibility, but it is not hidden from you that the meaning of iq'a' according to what he went to is contradictory to what passed that the correct is that what is meant is iq'a' in the first sense, so what is meant by iq'a' in the hadith of Abu Hurairah was not what is meant in the hadith of the 'heel of Satan', so there is no contradiction then, so no preponderance. I say: even if he dropped his statement 'and the commentator of al-Mughrib interpreted etc.', the answer would be correct without confusion, because what is meant by the permissive is what passed from Muslim and al-Bayhaqi, and by the preventive is the hadith prohibiting the 'heel of Satan', so it would be preponderant over the permissive without stopping at the condition that what is meant by the 'heel of Satan' is iq'a' according to al-Karkhi. So ponder. His statement: (and it is appropriate etc.) said in al-Nahr: 'It is only tanzihi on the second because this act is not iq'a'; rather, the dislike is due to leaving the sunnah sitting, as was reasoned in al-Bada'i'; and if iq'a' were interpreted by al-Karkhi's saying, the rulings would be reversed.' I say: it is not hidden from you what is in this speech, because each of the two acts is called iq'a', and the discussion is about what is meant in the hadith from them, as passed. So the correct thing is to say: 'It was only tanzihi on the second because this act is not what is meant in the hadith, i.e., it does not fall under the prohibition, and it is only disliked for leaving the sunnah sitting, so it becomes in it tahrimi because of the existence of the prohibition and leaving the sunnah sitting. And if iq'a' in the hadith were intended, then it would be tanzihi, unlike the first type, so it is that iq'a' according to al-Karkhi is the makruh tahrimi due to the existence of the two aforementioned matters, and the first is makruh tanzihi due to the absence of the prohibition.' And after this, there is also discussion because the 'heel of Satan' is iq'a' by al-Karkhi's interpretation, as the author mentioned from al-Mughrib, so it has been found. His statement: (and spreading the forearms) because of what is in Sahih Muslim from A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her): 'He - meaning the Prophet, peace be upon him - used to forbid that a man spread his forearms, that is the spreading of a wild beast.' And spreading them is placing them on the ground, as in al-Mughrib. It was said: He only forbade it because it is the characteristic of the lazy and careless in his condition, along with what is in it of resemblance to wild beasts and dogs.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on two disliked acts in prayer from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: al-iq'a' (sitting on the heels) and iftirash al-dhira'ayn (spreading the forearms on the ground). The author, al-Marghinani, provides proofs from hadith and discusses intra-Hanafi and inter-school differences.

For iq'a', the primary proof is the hadith prohibiting 'the sitting of the devil' (عقبة الشيطان) and the hadith in Musnad Ahmad that prohibits three things including 'iq'a' like a dog'. The author notes that this indicates karahah tahrimiyah (prohibited dislike) because the prohibition is explicit. Then he explains the difference of interpretation: most Hanafis, including the author of al-Hidayah, define iq'a' as sitting on the buttocks with knees erected (and some add hands on ground). Al-Karkhi defines it as sitting on the heels with feet erect and hands on ground. Both are makruh because they leave the sunnah sitting (jalsah sunniyyah). The hadith of Ibn Abbas and others that seem to permit sitting on the heels are either explained away as being outside prayer or as a different type of iq'a'. The Hanafi position is that both types are makruh, though the severity differs: the type that involves placing the buttocks on the ground with knees up is considered less severe (tanzihi), while the type described by al-Karkhi (sitting on heels) is considered tahrimi because it more directly resembles the prohibited 'heel of Satan'. The author resolves apparent contradictions by distinguishing between the two types and ruling: the first is makruh tanzihi for leaving the sunnah, the second is makruh tahrimi for explicit prohibition.

For iftirash al-dhira'ayn, the proof is the hadith of A'ishah that the Prophet forbade spreading the forearms on the ground like a wild beast. This is makruh because it resembles animals and indicates laziness. The ruling is established by the explicit prohibition and is considered makruh tanzihiy according to the Hanafi school, as it is not directly connected to an essential pillar but is a disliked posture.

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iq'a'): A sitting posture in prayer involving raising the knees or sitting on the heels; two types: (1) sitting on buttocks with knees erect, (2) sitting on heels with feet erect.
افتراش الذراعين (iftirash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms on the ground during sujud like a wild beast.
عقبة الشيطان ('uqbat al-shaytan): The 'heel of Satan', interpreted as a type of iq'a' (sitting on heels) prohibited in hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mustahabb
B) Makruh tanzihi
C) Makruh tahrimi
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules all forms of iq'a' and iftirash as makruh, with al-Karkhi's type of iq'a' being more severely disliked (tahrimi) due to the explicit prohibition.

Resolving Conflicting Hadith on Iqa' (Sitting on Heels)
Explaining the possible interpretations of the hadith on iqa' and the ruling on its prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqa' in prayer, explain the three methods of reconciling conflicting evidence, and apply the usul principle of preference for the preventing evidence.

Core Idea
When hadith conflict and dates are unknown, Hanafi usul prefers the preventing evidence (al-mani') over the permitting.

The Hanafi school teaches that sitting on the heels (iqa') in prayer is disliked, but the degree differs based on interpretation. This lesson explores how the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reconciles seemingly contradictory narrations using sound usul principles, specifically focusing on the principle of tarjih al-mani' ala al-mubih when tariq is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the conflicting evidence
The act is iqa' (sitting on the heels) during prayer. There are hadith indicating prohibition and others indicating permission. The student must recognize that conflicting evidence exists.
The hadith mentioned in the text is 'uqb al-shaytan' (heels of Satan), interpreted by al-Karkhi as iqa'.
2
Understand the three reconciliation methods
The author presents three possibilities: (1) carry the prohibiting hadith on an excuse (e.g., the person was in prayer despite illness), (2) carry it on being outside prayer, or (3) apply the usul principle that when preventing and permitting evidence conflict and the date is unknown, the preventing evidence is given preference.
The first two methods are historical interpretations; the third is a general usul rule.
3
Apply al-Karkhi's interpretation
The author of al-Mughrib (al-Karkhi) interprets 'heels of Satan' as iqa'. This makes the hadith a preventer (mani'), giving it precedence over any permitters (mubih).
This interpretation strengthens the prohibition.
4
Determine the hukm
Since the preventer outweighs the permitters, iqa' is disliked. However, note the nuance: under this interpretation, the dislike is makruh tanzihi (lesser), unlike another type of iqa' that scholars agree is more severely disliked (likely makruh tahrimi).
The text says: 'أن تكون كراهته تنزيهية بخلاف النوع المتفق على كراهته' – its dislike is tanzihi unlike the type agreed upon its dislike.
5
Note the usul principle
This case illustrates the Hanafi principle: when conflicting evidences (mani' and mubih) cannot be reconciled and the chronology is unknown, preference (tarjih) is given to the preventing evidence. This is a key rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
The principle is stated: 'إذا تعارضا ولم يعلم التاريخ كان الترجيح للمانع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها وينبغي إما بحمله على حالة العذر إن ثبت في بعض رواياته أنه كان في الصلاة أو بحمله على كونه خارج الصلاة إن لم يثبت أو لأن المانع والمبيح إذا تعارضا ولم يعلم التاريخ كان الترجيح للمانع. وقد فسر صاحب المغرب عقب الشيطان بالإقعاء عند الكرخي فكان مانعاً، أن تكون كراهته تنزيهية بخلاف النوع المتفق على كراهته
The Book of Prayer, Chapter on What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. And it is befitting either to carry it on the condition of an excuse if it is established in some of its narrations that he was in prayer, or to carry it on being outside prayer if not established, or because when the preventing and permitting evidence conflict and the date is unknown, preference is given to the preventing evidence. And the author of al-Mughrib interpreted the heels of Satan as iqa' according to al-Karkhi, so it is a preventer, that its dislike is tanzihi unlike the type agreed upon its dislike.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses the interpretation of the hadith prohibiting sitting on the heels (iqa') in prayer. The author presents three possible ways to reconcile contradictory narrations: either the hadith is carried on a case of excused (i.e., the person was in prayer despite illness), or it is carried on outside of prayer, or the principle of preferring the prohibiting evidence (al-mani') over the permitting evidence (al-mubih) when they conflict without known date. The author notes that according to al-Karkhi's interpretation, 'heels of Satan' refers to iqa', making the prohibition stronger. Consequently, the dislikedness of iqa' is tanzihi (lesser dislike) in this interpretation, as opposed to another type of iqa' which is agreed to be makruh (likely tahrimi). The commentary clarifies the hermeneutical principles used in Hanafi fiqh to resolve hadith conflicts.

Key Terms
إقعاء (iqa'): Sitting on the heels with the buttocks on the feet; the text discusses two interpretations of iqa'.
عقب الشيطان (uqb al-shaytan): Literally 'heels of Satan'; interpreted as a specific sitting posture (iqa') by al-Karkhi.
مانع (mani'): Preventer; evidence that prohibits an action, which takes precedence over permissive evidence when dates are unknown.
مبيح (mubih): Permitting evidence; evidence that allows an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Assume the later hadith abrogates the earlier.
B) Give preference to the preventing evidence (al-mani').
C) Follow the practice of the majority of companions.
D) Declare both hadith as weak.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on iqa' is makruh tanzihi under one interpretation, based on the usul principle of preferring the preventing evidence when dates are unknown.
