Prohibition of Spreading Forearms in Prayer (Iftirash al-Dhira'ayn)
Stating the prohibition with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iftirash al-dhira'ayn in prayer, identify the hadith proof, and explain why it is makruh tahrimi.

Core Idea
Spreading forearms in prayer like a beast is prohibited; the Prophet forbade it.

In this lesson, we examine a specific prohibited posture in prayer: spreading the forearms flat on the ground. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tahrimi based on a clear prohibition from the Prophet (ﷺ) recorded in Sahih Muslim. Understanding this ruling helps refine the posture of prayer and avoid resemblance to animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Iftirash al-dhira'ayn – spreading both forearms on the ground. What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
The source text states 'yinha' (he used to forbid), which indicates prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites a hadith from Sahih Muslim via Aisha (ra). Identify the exact wording of the prohibition.
The hadith is: 'He used to forbid a man from spreading his forearms, that is the spreading of a beast.'
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the dalil proves the ruling)
The imperative form of prohibition (yanha) and the comparison to a beast (iftirash al-sabu') both strengthen the prohibition. The Hanafi usul principle is that a command (or prohibition) in the imperative form indicates obligation/prohibition unless there is a contrary indicator.
The prohibition here is not absolute (haram) because the hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it becomes makruh tahrimi.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
The Hanafi school holds it as makruh tahrimi, while some other schools consider it only makruh tanzih. The difference stems from the weight given to the hadith and the principle of tashabbuh (imitation).
The practical effect: in the Hanafi school, repeating the prayer is not required but it is sinful to do so without excuse.
5
Extract the underlying rationale (ta'lil)
The text mentions two reasons: it is a posture of laziness and carelessness, and it involves resemblance to predatory beasts and dogs. This strengthens the prohibition because shari'ah discourages tashabbuh bi al-baha'im.
The ta'lil helps in analogizing to other similar postures, but the direct dalil is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وافترش ذراعيه) لما في صحيح عائشة مسلم عن . رضي الله عنها : وكان ـ يعني النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم - ينهى أن يفترش الرجل ذراعيه ذلك افتراش السبع. وافتراشهما إلقاؤهما على الأرض كما في المغرب. قيل: وإنما نهى عن لأنها صفة الكسلان والتهاون بحاله مع ما فيه من التشبه بالسباع والكلاب
His saying: 'and he spreads his forearms' — because what is in Sahih Muslim from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'He — meaning the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم — used to forbid a man from spreading his forearms, that is the spreading of a beast.' And their spreading is throwing them on the ground as in al-Mughrib. It is said: he only forbade it because it is the characteristic of the lazy and careless with his state, along with what it contains of resemblance to beasts and dogs.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh. It explains the phrase 'and he spreads his forearms' from the matn. The ruling is that spreading the forearms (placing them flat on the ground) during prayer is prohibited because the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم forbade it, as narrated in Sahih Muslim from Aisha. The act is likened to the spreading of a predatory beast, and also resembles the posture of lazy and careless people. The commentary also notes that the act involves tashabbuh (imitation) with animals, which is discouraged. The ruling is clear prohibition (nahi), and it is part of the makruhat of prayer.

Key Terms
افتراش الذراعين (iftirash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms on the ground during prayer, like a beast.
السبع (al-sabu'): Predatory beast; in the hadith, it refers to lions, etc.

The Hanafi Ruling on Sitting on Heels (Iqa') in Prayer
Detailed analysis of iqa' with hadith reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on iqa' between prostrations, distinguish its two types, explain how conflicting hadith are reconciled, and apply the ruling in practice.

Core Idea
The hadith of 'the heels of Satan' prohibits iqa' with erected knees; other forms are disliked, and permissive hadith are reinterpreted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the intricate discussion of sitting on the heels (iqa') during prayer. It presents the Hanafi position that iqa' is not permissible, and carefully resolves apparent contradictions among hadith by distinguishing between types of iqa' and contextualizing evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: one should not sit on the heels between prostrations but should sit as in normal prayer (iftirash).
This is the established zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Locate the conflicting evidence
Identify the hadith that seem to permit iqa' (from Muslim and al-Bayhaqi) and the hadith that prohibit it (the 'heels of Satan' hadith).
The permissive hadith are considered mubih (permitting), while the prohibiting hadith is a mani' (preventer).
3
Apply the tarjih principle
Explain the Hanafi usul principle that a preventer (mani') overrides a permissive (mubih). This resolves the apparent conflict by preferring the prohibition.
The 'heels of Satan' hadith is considered stronger in indicating prohibition.
4
Analyze the linguistic distinction
Distinguish the two types of iqa': type 1 (buttocks on heels, resting) and type 2 (knees erect, per al-Karkhi). Note that only type 2 corresponds to the 'heels of Satan' and is subject to the strict prohibition.
This distinction is crucial for the final ruling.
5
Determine the final ruling
Conclude that type 2 is makruh tahrimi (prohibited-disliked) due to the explicit prohibition, while type 1 is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked) for leaving the sunnah sitting.
The mufta bihi position is that iqa' with erected knees is tahrimi; the other form is tanzih.

His (al-Muwatta?) : He should not sit on his heels between the two prostrations, but he should sit between them as his sitting in his prayer, and this is the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and al-Tahawi mentioned it from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. Commentary
This is a detailed intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute about the ruling on sitting on the heels (iqa') between prostrations. The passage begins by stating the position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that one should not sit on his heels but rather sit as in his normal prayer (i.e., iftirash). It then discusses the interpretation of hadith that seem to permit iqa'. The author analyzes various attempts to reconcile the conflicting evidence, including carrying the permissive hadith on a case of excuse or outside prayer. The principle of preferring the preventing evidence (mani') over the permitting (mubih) is invoked. The commentary also examines the linguistic definitions of iqa' and the different types, ultimately concluding that the type of iqa' defined by al-Karkhi (erecting knees) is makruh tahrimi, while the other type (sitting on heels with buttocks on feet) is makruh tanzih. The passage ends with a final note that the prohibition of the 'heels of Satan' is tahrimi without an overriding factor.

Key Terms
إقعاء (iqa'): Sitting on the heels; the text distinguishes between two types: one with buttocks on heels (type 1) and one with knees erect (type 2 according to al-Karkhi).
عقب الشيطان (uqb al-shaytan): A posture interpreted as iqa' (knees erect), considered a preventer (mani') in hadith.
احتِباء (ihtiba'): Sitting with knees drawn up to the chest and wrapped with arms or cloth; not considered makruh outside prayer.

Brief ruling on returning salaam by hand during prayer being disliked.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Returning salaam by hand in prayer is makruh — it does not invalidate but is disliked.

In this lesson, we examine a brief yet important ruling from the Hanafi school: what happens when a person praying returns a greeting by hand gesture? The text tells us it is makruh and refers to the earlier discussion on invalidators. Understanding this ruling helps students distinguish between acts that invalidate prayer (mufsidat) and those that are merely disliked (makruh).

Hand gestures are unnecessary movements that conflict with the solemnity of prayer, but they do not reach the level of invalidating (mufsid).

Sitting Cross-Legged in Prayer Without Excuse: Makruh Tanzih
Stating the ruling and proof for sitting cross-legged in prayer without excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') in prayer without excuse and explain why it is makruh tanzih using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged in prayer abandons the sunnah, making it disliked, not because of resemblance to tyrants.

In the Hanafi school, the recommended sitting posture in prayer is iftirash (sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect). Sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') without a valid reason is considered makruh tanzih (lightly disliked) because it involves leaving the sunnah. Some have claimed it is disliked because it resembles tyrants, but the text refutes this by noting that the Prophet himself sat cross-legged outside prayer. We will examine the evidence and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') during the sitting portion of prayer (e.g., tashahhud). According to the matn, without excuse it is makruh. The commentator specifies it is makruh tanzih.
Makruh tanzih means lightly disliked, not prohibited.
2
Locate the reasoning (ta'lil)
The text states the reason: 'because it involves abandoning the sunnah of sitting in prayer.' This is the primary cause for the dislike.
The sunnah posture is iftirash.
3
Evaluate the false claim
Some claimed it is disliked because it resembles the sitting of tyrants (julus al-jababirah). The author refutes this by noting the Prophet and Umar sat cross-legged outside prayer, so the analogy is invalid.
The dislike is not due to tashabbuh (imitation) but due to abandoning sunnah.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar (in Bukhari) shows that Ibn Umar forbade tarbbu' and instructed the iftirash posture, but he himself did it due to weak legs. The hadith from Aisha (in Ibn Hibban) shows the Prophet praying cross-legged, which is interpreted as either for an excuse or to demonstrate permissibility.
The hadith from Aisha does not establish a general sunnah for cross-legged sitting.
5
Understand the condition of excuse
The ruling is qualified by 'without excuse'. If one has a valid excuse (e.g., leg injury), then it is not makruh, because even wajib acts can be left with excuse, so sunnah is even more deserving of leniency.
Excuse removes the dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( والتربع بلا عذر لأن فيه ترك سنة القعود في الصلاة. كذا علل به في الهداية وغيرها، وما قيل في وجه الكراهة أنه جلوس الجبابرة ليس بصحيح لأنه عليه السلام كان جل قعوده في غير الصلاة مع أصحابه التربع ، وكذا عمر رضي الله عنه . كذا ذكره المصنف وغيره . وتعليلهم بأن فيه ترك السنة يفيد أنه مكروه تنزيهاً إذ ليس فيه نهي خاص ليكون فيه تحريماً. وقيد بكونه بلا عذر لأنه ليس بمكروه تنزيهاً مع العذر لأن الواجب يترك مع العذر فالسنة أولى . وفي البخاري عن عبد الله بن عبد الله أنه كان يرى عبد الله بن عمر يتربع في الصلاة إذا جلس ففعلته وأنا يومئذ حديث السن فنهاني عبد الله بن عمر وقال : إنما سنة الصلاة أن تنصب رجلك اليمنى وتثني اليسرى فقلت : إنك تفعل ذلك ؟ فقال : إن رجلي لا يحملاني. وعليه يحمل ما في صحيح ابن حبان عن عائشة : رأيت النبي الله يصلي متربعاً. أو تعليماً للجواز. ثم الجلوس متربعاً معروف وإنما سمي بالتربع لأن صاحب هذه الجلسة قد ربع نفسه كما يربع الشيء إذا جعل أربعاً والأربع هنا الساقان والفخذان ربعها بمعنى أدخل بعضها تحت بعض
His saying: (and sitting cross-legged without excuse because it involves abandoning the sunnah of sitting in prayer. Thus it is reasoned in al-Hidayah and others. And what is said about the reason for its dislike that it is the sitting of tyrants is not correct, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, mostly sat cross-legged with his companions outside prayer, and likewise Umar, may Allah be pleased with him. The author and others mentioned that. Their reasoning that it abandons the sunnah indicates that it is makruh tanzih, since there is no specific prohibition to make it tahrimi. And it is conditioned by being without excuse, because it is not makruh tanzih when there is an excuse, since the obligatory can be left due to excuse, so the sunnah is more deserving. In al-Bukhari from Abdullah ibn Abdullah that he used to see Abdullah ibn Umar sitting cross-legged in prayer when he sat, so he did it while he was young, but Abdullah ibn Umar forbade him and said: 'The sunnah of prayer is to erect your right leg and bend your left.' He said: 'But you do that?' He replied: 'My legs do not carry me.' And upon this is carried what is in Sahih Ibn Hibban from Aisha: 'I saw the Prophet praying sitting cross-legged.' Or it is a teaching of permissibility. Then sitting cross-legged is well-known, and it is called tarbbu' because the person has made himself four, as a thing is made four when it is made four, and the four here are the two shins and two thighs, meaning he interlaces them.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding sitting cross-legged (tarbbu') in prayer without an excuse. The matn states it is disliked, and the sharh explains the reasoning: it abandons the sunnah sitting posture (iftirash). The author refutes the claim that it resembles the sitting of tyrants, noting that the Prophet himself sat cross-legged outside prayer. Therefore, the dislike is tanzih (lightly disliked) because there is no explicit prohibition. It is conditioned by the absence of an excuse; if one has an excuse (e.g., weak legs), then it is not disliked. The hadith from Bukhari shows that Abdullah ibn Umar forbade it because it is against the sunnah, but he himself did it due to weakness. Another hadith from Aisha where the Prophet prayed cross-legged is interpreted as either teaching permissibility or indicating a valid excuse. The linguistic origin of tarbbu' is also explained.

Key Terms
التربع (al-tarbbu'): Sitting cross-legged with shins and thighs interwoven.
جلوس الجبابرة (julus al-jababirah): Sitting of tyrants; some claimed tarbbu' resembles that, but the text refutes this.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is a posture of the Jews.
C) Because it abandons the sunnah posture of iftirash.

Twisting Hair in Prayer: Prohibition and Proof
Stating the prohibition of twisting hair with hadith proof and wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on twisting hair in prayer, cite the hadith proof, explain the wisdom behind the prohibition, and identify the scope of 'aqs al-sha'r.

Core Idea
Twisting hair in prayer is prohibited because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the person.

In this passage, we examine a specific ruling regarding the grooming of hair during salah. The Hanafi school holds that twisting or gathering the hair on the head before entering prayer is makruh tahrimi. The evidence comes from a prophetic command forbidding the folding of hair and garment during prostration. This lesson unpacks the proof, the wisdom, and the nuances of what constitutes 'aqs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State what 'aqs al-sha'r means and its hukm (makruh tahrimi).
The passage explicitly says 'الظاهر أن الكراهة تحريمية'.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith from the six books and the Muslim narration. Identify the key phrase: 'لا أكف شعراً ولا ثوباً'.
The hadith is the primary proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the prohibition indicates tahrim without a sarf (overriding factor).
The nahy is explicit and unqualified.
4
Understand the wisdom
Why is this prohibited? The hadith comparison to bound hands shows it resembles a restriction; the scholars say the hair should prostrate with the person.
The wisdom is given: 'أن الشعر يسجد معه'.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
The jurists differed on the exact method of 'aqs. The Hanafi position covers all forms.
The text lists three opinions on the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وعقص شعره) أي عقص شعر الرأس فيها بمعنى أن يفعل ذلك قبل الدخول فيها ثم يدخل كذلك لما روى أصحاب الكتب الستة عنه الا الله أنه قال «أمرت أن أسجد على سبعة وأن لا أكف شعراً ولا ثوباً» (۱) وفي العقص كفه ما رواه مسلم عن كريب أن ابن عباس رأى عبد الله بن الحرث يصلي ورأسه معقوص من ورائه فجعل يحله فلما انصرف قال : مالك ولرأسي؟ قال : إني سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول إنما مثل هذا مثل الذي يصلي وهو مكتوف» (۲) ولهذا قال العلماء : حكمة النهي . عنه أن الشعر يسجد معه . والظاهر أن الكراهة تحريمية للنهي المذكور بلا صارف، ولا فرق فيه بين أن يتعمده للصلاة أو لا وهو في اللغة جمع الشعر على الرأس ، وقيل ليه وإدخال أطرافه في أصواله . كذا في المغرب. واختلف الفقهاء فيه على أقوال : فقيل أن يجمعه وسط رأسه ثم يشده، وقيل أن يلف ذوائبه حول رأسه كما يفعله النساء، وقيل أن يجمعه من قبل القفا ويمسكه بخيط
His saying: (and twisting his hair) i.e., twisting the hair of the head in it, meaning that he does that before entering into it (prayer) then enters like that. Because the narrators of the six books narrated from him that he said: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven (bones) and not to fold up hair or garment.' And in twisting, its prevention is what Muslim narrated from Kuraib that Ibn Abbas saw Abdullah ibn al-Harith praying with his hair twisted behind him, so he started to undo it. When he finished, he said: 'What is it with you and my hair?' He said: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah say: The like of this is like the one who prays while his hands are tied.' For this reason the scholars said: The wisdom of the prohibition is that the hair should prostrate with him. And it appears that the dislike is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) due to the mentioned prohibition without any overriding factor, and there is no difference whether he intends it for prayer or not. And it is in language: gathering the hair on the head, and it is said: winding and inserting its ends into its roots. Thus in al-Mughrib. And the jurists differed about it into opinions: some said he gathers it in the middle of his head then ties it, some said he wraps his locks around his head as women do, and some said he gathers it from the back of the neck and holds it with a thread.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on twisting or gathering the hair ('aqs al-sha'r) during prayer. It is considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because the Prophet explicitly forbade folding up the hair and garment while praying. The wisdom is that the hair should prostrate with the person, i.e., it should be in its natural state and not prevented from touching the ground during prostration. Two hadith are cited: one from the 'six books' stating the command to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garment, and another from Muslim where Ibn Abbas rebukes Abdullah ibn al-Harith for praying with twisted hair, comparing it to praying with hands tied. The text also notes that there is no difference between doing it intentionally for prayer or not; it is still disliked. The linguistic definition of 'aqs is given as gathering the hair on the head, and the jurists have different opinions on the exact method of twisting.

Key Terms
عقص الشعر ('aqs al-sha'r): Twisting or gathering the hair on the head, especially in prayer.
كف الشعر (kaff al-sha'r): Folding up or gathering the hair; the hadith prohibits kaff al-sha'r and al-thawb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a form of adornment.
B) Because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the person.
C) Because it resembles the disbelievers.
D) Because it is an unnecessary movement.

Key Takeaway
Twisting hair in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it prevents the hair from prostrating, as proven by the Prophet's prohibition.

The Ruling of Iqa' According to Al-Karkhi
Concluding ruling on iqa' according to al-Karkhi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for iqa' (sitting with knees erect) according to al-Karkhi and identify the exception to the prohibition.

Core Idea
Iqa' is makruh tahrimi unless a clear factor shifts it to mere dislike.

This passage concludes the discussion on iqa' — the posture of sitting with knees erect during prayer. Al-Karkhi's interpretation defines iqa' as the 'heels of Satan', which is prohibited in hadith. The text firmly establishes that this posture is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) regardless of how it relates to other hadiths, and only an overriding factor can reduce its severity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Recognize that iqa' according to al-Karkhi refers to sitting with knees erect, also known as the 'heels of Satan'.
2
State the default hukm
The default ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it is prohibited in the hadith.
This is the relied-upon position.
3
Note the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whether or not this iqa' matches the specific wording of Abu Hurayra's hadith.
The prohibition stands on its own.
4
Identify the exception
If there is a valid factor (such as a legitimate excuse or contrary evidence) that overrides the prohibition, the ruling may shift from tahrimi to nadb (recommendation) or lesser degrees.
This exception is rarely applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Or iqa' according to this interpretation is both of the matters also, because the heels of Satan are also prohibited as has passed, so iqa' according to the interpretation of al-Karkhi is makruh tahrimi, whether it is the intended meaning of the hadith of Abu Hurayra or not, unless there is a factor that turns the prohibition from tahrimi to nadb (recommendation).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is a concluding note on the ruling of iqa' (sitting with knees erect) according to al-Karkhi's interpretation. It reinforces that this posture is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because it is the 'heels of Satan' which is prohibited in hadith. The prohibition applies regardless of whether this is the same iqa' mentioned in Abu Hurayra's hadith. The only exception is if there is a clear factor that reduces the prohibition to mere dislike (tanzih) or recommendation (nadb). This passage ties together the earlier discussion and settles the ruling for the type of iqa' defined by al-Karkhi.

Key Terms
الإقعاء عند الكرخي (al-iqa' 'inda al-Karkhi): Sitting with knees erect, i.e., the posture referred to as 'heels of Satan'.
عقبة الشيطان (uqbat al-shaytan): The 'heels of Satan' – a prohibited sitting posture in prayer.

The Ruling on Iq'a' (Sitting on Heels) in Prayer
Determines the ruling on iq'a' based on its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on iq'a' according to the Hanafi school and identify the basis for its prohibition.

Core Idea
Iq'a' is makruh tahrimi because it resembles the sitting of the devil, which is prohibited.

This lesson examines the ruling on iq'a'—a specific sitting posture in prayer. According to the Hanafi school, particularly al-Karkhi's interpretation, iq'a' is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). The prohibition stems from its resemblance to the sitting of the devil (qafzat al-shaytan), which has been explicitly prohibited in hadith. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the conditions that might affect it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
What is iq'a'? There are two interpretations: sitting on the heels with buttocks on feet, or sitting on the feet with knees raised. Both are considered.
The passage focuses on al-Karkhi's interpretation.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to al-Karkhi, iq'a' is ruled as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
3
Locate the dalil
The prohibition of resembling the devil (qafzat al-shaytan) comes from a hadith of Abu Huraira.
The hadith is ahad, so thubut is zanni.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of the devil's sitting indicates that any similar posture is also prohibited unless there is evidence to the contrary.
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition (nahy) indicates tahrim unless there is a qarinah.
5
Note the exception
The ruling applies unless there is a factor that diverts the prohibition from tahrim to recommendation (nadb).
For example, a valid excuse like illness might change the ruling.

This passage is a ruling on iq'a' (sitting on the heels with the buttocks on the feet) in prayer. The ruling applies regardless of whether this particular form is exactly what is meant by the hadith of Abu Huraira, unless there is some factor that changes it from prohibition to mere recommendation (i.e., if there is a valid excuse or evidence).

عقبة الشيطان (qafzat al-shaytan): A type of sitting resembling a dog or devil, prohibited in prayer.

Ruling on I'tijar (Wrapping Turban Exposing Head)
Explains the ruling and definitions of i'tijar in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of i'tijar in Hanafi fiqh and identify the reasons for its dislike.

Core Idea
I'tijar is disliked in prayer because it resembles the People of the Book or the wicked, per Hanafi sources.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a specific turban style called i'tijar. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq quotes multiple authorities explaining why it is makruh. You will learn the definition, the evidence, and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is i'tijar? The text gives several definitions: not wrapping the turban leaving the crown exposed, covering the nose like a niqab, etc. The ruling is consistently makruh (disliked).
2
Locate the dalil
What proofs are cited? The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it (hadith) and Ibn Abbas said a man should not cover his nose while praying.
These are ahad hadith, so zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the prohibition indicate dislike? The prohibition is not absolute but understood as tanzihi (disliked) due to the zanni nature of the evidence and the comparison to resemblance.
4
Note the reason for dislike
Multiple reasons: resembling ruffians, the wicked, the People of the Book, or arrogance. Imam al-Walwaliji sums it up as resemblance to the People of the Book.
The text states it is disliked even if done for heat or cold.
5
Evaluate the scope
Does the ruling apply only in prayer? Al-Walwaliji says it is disliked outside prayer, so inside it is even more disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية ويكره الاعتجار وهو لا لف العمامة حول رأسه وإبداء الهامة كما يفعله الشطار اهـ. وفي المحيط ويكره الاعتجار لأنه عليه السلام نهى عنه وهو أن يكور عمامته ويترك وسط رأسه مكشوفاً كهيئة الأشرار . وقيل : أن يتنقب بعمامته فيغطي أنفه كمعجز النساء إما لأجل الحر أو البرد أو للتكبر وهو مكروه لقول ابن عباس : لا يغطي الرجل وهو يصلي .اهـ. وفي المغرب : وتفسير من قال هو أن يلف العمامة على رأسه ويبدي الهامة أقرب لأنه مأخوذ من معجر المرأة وهو ثوب كالعصابة تلفه المرأة على استدارة رأسها اهـ. والمعجر على وزن منبر وعلل كراهة الاعتجار الإمام الولوالجي بأنه تشبه بأهل الكتاب قال : وهو مكروه خارج الصلاة ففيها أولى
In al-Dhahiriyyah: It is disliked (makruh) i'tijar, which is not wrapping the turban around the head and exposing the crown of the head as the ruffians do. In al-Muhit: It is disliked because he (peace be upon him) forbade it, and it is that he folds his turban and leaves the middle of his head uncovered like the appearance of the wicked. It is also said: That he covers his nose with his turban like the niqab of women, either due to heat or cold or out of arrogance, and it is disliked due to the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'A man should not cover [his nose] while praying.' In al-Mughrib: The interpretation of those who say it is to wrap the turban around the head and expose the crown is closer because it is derived from the women's mi'jar, which is a garment like a headband that a woman wraps around her head. Imam al-Walwaliji explained the dislike of i'tijar as resemblance to the People of the Book, saying: it is disliked outside prayer, so inside it is even more [disliked].
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage describes the ruling on i'tijar, which is a particular way of wearing the turban leaving the crown of the head exposed. Multiple sources (al-Dhahiriyyah, al-Muhit) state it is disliked because it imitates ruffians or the wicked, and the Prophet forbade it. Another form is covering the nose like a niqab, which is also disliked by the saying of Ibn Abbas. The linguistic connection to the women's mi'jar is mentioned, and Imam al-Walwaliji adds the reason of resemblance to the People of the Book, making it disliked even outside prayer, so more so during prayer.

Key Terms
الاعتجار (al-i'tijar): Wrapping the turban leaving the crown of the head exposed, or covering the nose with it.
المعجر (al-mi'jar): A garment like a headband that women wrap around the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is forbidden by ijma
D) It is a sunnah of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
I'tijar is disliked in Hanafi prayer because it resembles the People of the Book or the wicked, but the prayer remains valid.

The Ruling on Kaf al-Thawb (Holding the Garment) in Prayer
Establishes the ruling and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kaf al-thawb, identify its basis in the hadith, and explain the reason for its dislike.

Core Idea
Folding or holding the garment in prayer is makruh because it contradicts the sunnah and resembles the People of the Book.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on kaf al-thawb, which includes folding, tying, or tucking the garment during prayer. The author cites the prophetic hadith on prostration and identifies the practice as disliked (makruh) due to its conflict with the sunnah and its resemblance to non-Muslim practices. Let us unpack the evidence and the general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kaf al-thawb (holding/tucking the garment) during prayer. The ruling is that it is makruh (disliked).
Includes folding from front or back when going down for prostration.
2
Locate the evidence
The proof comes from a previous hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) was commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold his hair or garment.
Hadith reference is from the earlier part of the text.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah (point of evidence)
The hadith explicitly prohibits folding the garment, and the Hanafi school extends this to any form of kaf because the same reasoning (tark al-sunnah) applies.
The sunnah is to release the hands and not gather the garment.
4
Note the exceptions
The passage later mentions exceptions (e.g., lifting to avoid dust), but the general rule remains makruh.
Exceptions are covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وكف ثويه للحديث السابق سواء كان من بين يديه أو خلفه عند الانحطاط للسجود والكف هو الضم والجمع ولأن فيه ترك سنة اليد، وذكر في المغرب عن بعضهم أن الائتزار فوق القميص من الكف .اهـ. فعلى هذا يكره أن يصلي مشدود الوسط فوق القميص ونحوه أيضاً وقد صرح به في العتابية معلللاً بأنه صنيع أهل الكتاب لكن في الخلاصة أنه لا يكره . كذا في شرح منية المصلي. ويدخل أيضاً في كف الثوب تشمير كميه كما في فتح القدير وظاهره الإطلاق وفي الخلاصة ومنية المصلي قيد الكراهة بأن يكون رافعاً كميه إلى المرفقين وظاهره أنه لا يكره إذا كان يرفعهما إلى ما دونهما والظاهر الإطلاق لصدق كف الثوب على الكل. وذكر في المجتبى في كراهة تشمير الكمين قولين، وذكر في القنية أن القول بإمساك الكمين أحوط ولا يخفي ما فيه . وفي مذهب مالك تفصيل قد كنت رأيته لأئمتنا في بعض الفتاوى ولم يحضرني تعيينها الآن وهو أنه يكره إن كان للصلاة لا إذا كان لأجل شغل ثم حضرته الصلاة فصلى وهو على تلك الهيئة ومن كف الثوب رفعه كيلا يتترب كما في منية المصلي، وقيل لا بأس بصونه عن التراب كما في المجتبى .
His statement: And holding (kaf) his garment (thawb) due to the previous hadith, whether from in front of him or behind him when going down for prostration. Kaf means folding and gathering, and because it involves leaving the sunnah of the hand. It is mentioned in al-Mughrib from some that wearing the izaar (lower garment) over the qamis (shirt) is a form of kaf. Hence, it is disliked to pray with the waist tied tightly over the qamis and similar, and it is explicitly stated in al-Attabiyyah, reasoned that it is the practice of the People of the Book. However, in al-Khulasah it is not disliked. Similarly in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. Included in kaf al-thawb is rolling up the sleeves as in Fath al-Qadir; apparently it is absolute. In al-Khulasah and Munyat al-Musalli, the dislike is restricted to raising the sleeves to the elbows, and apparently it is not disliked if raised less than that. The absolute is used because kaf al-thawb applies to all. In al-Mujtaba, there are two opinions on the dislike of rolling up the sleeves. In al-Qunyah, it is said that holding the sleeves is more cautious, and what is in it is not hidden. In the Maliki school, there is a detail which I have seen from our imams in some fatawa but I do not recall its specification now: it is disliked if for the prayer, not if due to work then he prays in that state. And from kaf al-thawb is lifting it so as not to get dusty, as in Munyat al-Musalli; and it is said there is no harm in protecting it from dust, as in al-Mujtaba.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on kaf al-thawb (holding the garment) in prayer, based on the hadith prohibiting folding hair and garment. It includes rolling up the sleeves or tying the waist. The general position is that it is disliked because it involves leaving the sunnah, and it resembles the practice of the People of the Book. There are differences: some say it is not disliked (Khulasah), others restrict dislike to raising sleeves to the elbows. The author also mentions the Maliki school's detail and the case of lifting the garment to avoid dust.

Key Terms
كف الثوب (kaf al-thawb): Holding, folding, or tucking the garment during prayer, including rolling sleeves or tying the waist.
الائتزار فوق القميص (al-i'tizar fawqa al-qamis): Wearing the lower garment (izaar) over the shirt, considered a form of kaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the dislike of kaf al-thawb (holding the garment) in prayer?
A) It shows disrespect
B) The hadith prohibits folding hair or garment
C) It is an innovation
D) It distracts from khushu

Key Takeaway
Kaf al-thawb is makruh because the Prophet prohibited folding the garment in prayer.

Intra-Madhhab Differences and Exceptions in Kaf al-Thawb
Explains details and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the different opinions on rolling up sleeves and lifting the garment, and apply the exception for necessity like avoiding dust.

Core Idea
The dislike of kaf al-thawb has exceptions: lifting to avoid dust is permissible, and there is disagreement on rolling sleeves up to the elbows.

The previous lesson established the general ruling. This lesson delves into the details: Is rolling up the sleeves included? Is lifting the garment to protect it from dust also disliked? The passage presents multiple opinions and an important exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sub-issues
The passage discusses: rolling up sleeves (tashmir al-kumm), wearing izaar over qamis, lifting garment to avoid dust.
These are all considered forms of kaf al-thawb or related.
2
Classify the acts
Determine which are considered kaf al-thawb and which are not.
Rolling sleeves is kaf; lifting to avoid dust may be an exception.
3
Apply the rulings
For each case, state whether it is makruh or not, citing the source authorities.
E.g., rolling sleeves to elbows: disputed; below elbows: not disliked according to some.
4
Evaluate the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Compare the views of al-Khulasah, al-Mujtaba, al-Qunyah, and the mufta bihi.
The mufta bihi leans towards general dislike, but with leniency.

Refining the Ruling on Rolling Up Sleeves (Tashmir al-Kummayn)
Super-commentary refining the ruling on rolling up sleeves during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the refined Hanafi ruling on tashmir al-kummayn and apply the intention-based distinction to determine when the action is disliked.

Core Idea
Rolling up sleeves is disliked only if done for prayer, not if from prior work.

This lesson examines a refined Hanafi ruling on rolling up sleeves (tashmir al-kummayn) during prayer. The super-commentary resolves an apparent contradiction between an absolute statement from Fath al-Qadir and later qualifications from al-Kamal and other sources. The key insight is that the ruling depends on the intention: if sleeves are rolled up specifically for prayer, it is disapproved; but if one prays with sleeves already rolled up from prior work, it is not disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Initial Absolute Ruling
The passage begins by referencing an apparent absolute statement from Fath al-Qadir that rolling up sleeves is disliked. Note that this is the starting point that needs refinement.
2
Locate the Refinement and Its Source
Al-Kamal's later qualification is cited: 'It is also disliked to pray with the sleeve above the forearm.' This specification reconciles with al-Khulasah and al-Munyah, showing that the ruling is not absolute.
3
Understand the Intention-Based Reasoning
From al-Qunyah and Najm al-A'imma, the passage introduces a distinction based on intention. If sleeves were rolled up for prior work (or habit), the dislike is less certain; if rolled up for the prayer itself, it is clearly disliked.
4
Apply the Refined Ruling to a Scenario
Consider a person who enters the mosque with sleeves already rolled up from gardening. According to the refined ruling, he may pray without adjustment; his action is not disliked. The key is that the rolling was not done for the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والظاهر الإطلاق فيه نظر إن يكن سنده ما ذكره عن فتح القدير لأن الكمال وإن أطلق هنا قد قيد كلامه فيما بعد عند استطراد فروع ذكرها فقال : وتكره الصلاة أيضاً مع الكم عن الساعد فلا مخالفة بينه وبين الخلاصة والمنية . كذا في الشرنبلالية تأمل قوله : (وفي مذهب مالك تفصيل الخ) قال في النهر المذكور في القنية أنه لو شمر كميه لعمل كان يعمله قبل الصلاة اختلفوا في الكراهة وهو ظاهر في الكراهة فيما لو شمر لها .اهـ وعبارة القنية : واختلف فيمن صلى وقد شمر كميه لعمل كان يعمله قبل الصلاة أو هيئته ذلك. وفيها أيضاً عن نجم الأئمة: وكان
His statement: (And the apparent absolute [interpretation] requires consideration if its basis is what he mentioned from Fath al-Qadir, because al-Kamal although he made it absolute here, later qualified his words when discussing ramifications, saying: 'It is also disliked to pray with the sleeve above the forearm.' So there is no contradiction between him and al-Khulasah and al-Munyah. Thus in al-Shurnublaliyyah. Consider his statement: (And in the Maliki school a detail...). He said in al-Nahr: In al-Qunyah, if he rolled up his sleeves for work he was doing before prayer, they differed on dislike; it is apparent that it is disliked if he rolled them up for [the prayer]. The wording of al-Qunyah: They differed concerning one who prayed having rolled up his sleeves for work he was doing before prayer or as his habit. And in it also from Najm al-A'imma: And it was...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary that refines the previous discussion on rolling up sleeves (tashmir al-kummayn) in prayer. The author notes that an apparent absolute ruling from Fath al-Qadir must be considered carefully because al-Kamal later specified that it is disliked to pray with the sleeve above the forearm. This reconciles with al-Khulasah and al-Munyah. The Maliki school's detail is mentioned: dislike depends on intention - not disliked if done for prior work, but disliked if done for the prayer itself. The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
تشمير الكمين (tashmir al-kummayn): Rolling up the sleeves, especially above the elbow.
الشرنبلالية (al-Shurnublaliyyah): A later Hanafi commentary work by al-Shurnublali.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The refined Hanafi ruling on tashmir al-kummayn depends on intention: disliked if for the prayer, not disliked if from prior work.

Ruling on Sadl (Letting Garment Hang) in Prayer
Defines sadl and gives its ruling and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sadl in prayer, identify its two definitions, and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Letting the garment hang loose (sadl) in prayer is makruh due to the Prophet's prohibition.

This lesson examines the ruling of sadl—allowing one's garment to hang loose without folding the sides—during prayer. The Hanafi school considers it disliked (makruh) based on a sahih hadith. We will also explore the exact definition of sadl as clarified by early Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that sadl is makruh because the Prophet prohibited it.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim, authenticated, is the proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition (nahy) indicates dislike (karahah).
4
Note the definition of sadl
Two definitions are given: letting the garment hang without folding the sides, or throwing it over the head and letting it loose on the shoulders.
5
Note al-Karkhi's specific case
Al-Karkhi adds: placing the garment on head or shoulders and letting ends hang when not wearing trousers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وسدله) لنهيه عليه السلام عنه كما أخرجه أبو داود والحاكم وصححه. يقال سدل الثوب سدلاً من باب طلب إذا أرسله من غير أن يضم جانبه . وقيل : هو أن يلقيه على رأسه ويرخيه على منكبيه وأسدل خطأ . كذا في المغرب. وذكر في البدائع أن الكرخي فسره بأن يجعل ثوبه على رأسه أو على كتفيه ويرسل أطرافه من جوانبه إذا لم يكن عليه سراويل.
His statement: (And sadl) due to the prohibition of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim and they authenticated it. It is said: 'sadala al-thawb a sadl' from the form 'talaba' when he lets it down without folding its side. It is also said: That he throws it over his head and lets it loose on his shoulders, and 'asdala' is a mistake. Thus in al-Mughrib. It is mentioned in al-Bada'i' that al-Karkhi interpreted it as placing his garment on his head or on his shoulders and sending its ends down from his sides if he is not wearing trousers.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines sadl (letting the garment hang loose) and gives its ruling: it is disliked because the Prophet prohibited it, as recorded by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim. Two definitions are given: (1) letting the garment down without folding the sides, (2) throwing it over the head and letting it loose on the shoulders. Al-Karkhi adds a specific case: placing the garment on head or shoulders and letting the ends hang if one is not wearing trousers. The ruling is disliked due to the hadith.

Key Terms
سدل الثوب (sadl al-thawb): Allowing the garment to hang loose without folding the sides, or throwing it over the head and letting it down on the shoulders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a bid'ah
B) The Prophet prohibited it
C) It is uncomfortable
D) Ijma of the Sahaba

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers sadl in prayer makruh due to the Prophet's prohibition; it is defined as letting the garment hang without folding or throwing it over the head and shoulders.

Brief List of Disliked Acts in Prayer
Summarizes several disliked actions in prayer with a single ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five disliked acts mentioned in the passage and state their common ruling (makruh).

Core Idea
Yawning, closing eyes, and praying alone on a mat are disliked, not haram.

This passage from Ghayat al-Bayan presents a quick reference list of actions considered makruh (disliked) during prayer. While these actions do not invalidate the prayer, they reduce its perfection. Recognizing them helps you avoid them in your salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify each action
Read the Arabic passage and note each specific action: sadl (letting the garment hang), yawning, closing the eyes, the imam not prostrating inside the prayer niche, and the imam praying alone on a mat.
Remember the Arabic terms: التثاؤب, تغميض العينين, etc.
2
Confirm the ruling
The text explicitly says 'وكل ذلك مكروه' – all of that is makruh. Understand that makruh in Hanafi fiqh usually means disliked but not prohibited; it does not break the prayer.
Makruh tanzihan (slightly disliked) is the default here.
3
Recognize the source
The passage ends with 'كذا في غاية البيان' – thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. This shows the authority of the ruling within the Hanafi school.
Ghayat al-Bayan is a reliable Hanafi reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسدله والتثاؤب وتغميض عينيه وقيام الإمام لا سجوده في الطاق وانفراد الإمام على خرقة وكل ذلك مكروه . كذا في غاية البيان
And sadl, yawning, closing his eyes, the imam not prostrating in the arch, and the imam being alone on a mat - all of that is disliked (makruh). Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This is a ruling statement listing several actions that are disliked in prayer: sadl (letting garment hang), yawning, closing the eyes, the imam not prostrating within the prayer niche (tāq), and the imam standing alone on a prayer mat while the followers are elsewhere. The source is Ghayat al-Bayan.

Key Terms
التثاؤب (al-tatha'ub): Yawning during prayer.
تغميض العينين (taghmid al-'aynayn): Closing the eyes in prayer.
قيام الإمام لا سجوده في الطاق (qiyam al-imam la sujudahu fi al-taq): The imam stands to pray but does not prostrate inside the prayer niche (mihrab).
انفراد الإمام على خرقة (infirad al-imam 'ala kharqah): The imam prays alone on a mat (while followers are separate).
سدل (sadl): Letting the garment hang loosely without gathering it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Closing the eyes

Key Takeaway
Five specific actions in prayer are makruh (disliked) but do not invalidate the prayer: sadl, yawning, closing eyes, imam not prostrating in the niche, and imam standing alone on a mat.

Ruling on al-Ihtijar and al-Sadl in Prayer
Commentary expanding on disliked actions in prayer with definitions and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ihtijar and al-sadl, state their rulings in prayer, and explain the Hanafi proofs and intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Ihtijar and sadl are makruh because they resemble the People of the Book and may expose the 'awra.

This lesson covers two disliked actions in prayer: ihtijar (wrapping the turban while leaving the crown exposed) and sadl (letting the garment hang loose or throwing it over the head). Both have been explicitly prohibited by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and are considered makruh tanzihi or tahrimi by different authorities. The underlying rationale (ta'lil) includes resemblance to non-Muslims and risk of uncovering the 'awra during bowing.

Guided Path
1
Define the act
Identify the precise definition of ihtijar and sadl from the text. Ihtijar is wrapping the turban exposing the crown; sadl is letting the garment hang without folding the side or throwing it over the head.
Note that sadl also includes wearing a cloak without inserting hands into sleeves.
2
Identify the proof
Locate the hadith or statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that prohibits these actions. For ihtijar, the Prophet forbade it; for sadl, the hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim.
The text also cites the saying of Ibn Abbas.
3
Understand the ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Explain why these actions are disliked. The text mentions resemblance to the People of the Book (tashabbuh bi ahl al-kitab) and potential exposure of the 'awra.
Imam al-Walwaliji explicitly says ihtijar is disliked because of resemblance to the People of the Book.
4
Note intra-Hanafi differences
Observe that there is disagreement on sadl outside prayer: some scholars consider it disliked, while the authenticated view in al-Quniyya is that it is not disliked.
Inside prayer, all agree it is disliked.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
State the operative ruling for fatwa: ihtijar is makruh both inside and outside prayer; sadl is makruh in prayer but not disliked outside prayer according to the chosen view.
The tarjih authority is Ibn Nujaym and later Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها وسدله والتثاؤب وتغميض عينيه وقيام الإمام لا سجوده في الطاق وانفراد الإمام على خرقة وكل ذلك مكروه . كذا في غاية البيان وفي الظهيرية ويكره الاعتجار وهو لف العمامة حول رأسه وإبداء الهامة كما يفعله الشطار اهـ. وفي المحيط ويكره الاعتجار لأنه عليه السلام نهى عنه وهو أن يكور عمامته ويترك وسط رأسه مكشوفاً كهيئة الأشرار . وقيل : أن يتنقب بعمامته فيغطي أنفه كمعجز النساء إما لأجل الحر أو البرد أو للتكبر وهو مكروه لقول ابن عباس : لا يغطي الرجل وهو يصلي .اهـ. وفي المغرب : وتفسير من قال هو أن يلف العمامة على رأسه ويبدي الهامة أقرب لأنه مأخوذ من معجر المرأة وهو ثوب كالعصابة تلفه المرأة على استدارة رأسها اهـ. والمعجر على وزن منبر وعلل كراهة الاعتجار الإمام الولوالجي بأنه تشبه بأهل الكتاب قال : وهو مكروه خارج الصلاة ففيها أولى قوله وكف ثويه للحديث السابق سواء كان من بين يديه أو خلفه عند الانحطاط للسجود والكف هو الضم والجمع ولأن فيه ترك سنة اليد، وذكر في المغرب عن بعضهم أن الائتزار فوق القميص من الكف .اهـ. فعلى هذا يكره أن يصلي مشدود الوسط فوق القميص ونحوه أيضاً وقد صرح به في العتابية معلللاً بأنه صنيع أهل الكتاب لكن في الخلاصة أنه لا يكره . كذا في شرح منية المصلي. ويدخل أيضاً في كف الثوب تشمير كميه كما في فتح القدير وظاهره الإطلاق وفي الخلاصة ومنية المصلي قيد الكراهة بأن يكون رافعاً كميه إلى المرفقين وظاهره أنه لا يكره إذا كان يرفعهما إلى ما دونهما والظاهر الإطلاق لصدق كف الثوب على الكل. وذكر في المجتبى في كراهة تشمير الكمين قولين، وذكر في القنية أن القول بإمساك الكمين أحوط ولا يخفي ما فيه . وفي مذهب مالك تفصيل قد كنت رأيته لأئمتنا في بعض الفتاوى ولم يحضرني تعيينها الآن وهو أنه يكره إن كان للصلاة لا إذا كان لأجل شغل ثم حضرته الصلاة فصلى وهو على تلك الهيئة ومن كف الثوب رفعه كيلا يتترب كما في منية المصلي، وقيل لا بأس بصونه عن التراب كما في المجتبى . من قوله (وسدله) لنهيه عليه السلام عنه كما أخرجه أبو داود والحاكم وصححه. . يقال سدل الثوب سدلاً من باب طلب إذا أرسله من غير أن يضم جانبه . وقيل : هو أن يلقيه على رأسه ويرخيه على منكبيه وأسدل خطأ . كذا في المغرب. وذكر في البدائع أن الكرخي فسره بأن يجعل ثوبه على رأسه أو على كتفيه ويرسل أطرافه من جوانبه إذا لم يكن عليه سراويل. تشمیر قوله : (والظاهر الإطلاق فيه نظر إن يكن سنده ما ذكره عن فتح القدير لأن الكمال وإن أطلق هنا قد قيد كلامه فيما بعد عند استطراد فروع ذكرها فقال : وتكره الصلاة أيضاً مع الكم عن الساعد فلا مخالفة بينه وبين الخلاصة والمنية . كذا في الشرنبلالية تأمل قوله : (وفي مذهب مالك تفصيل الخ) قال في النهر المذكور في القنية أنه لو شمر كميه لعمل كان يعمله قبل الصلاة اختلفوا في الكراهة وهو ظاهر في الكراهة فيما لو شمر لها .اهـ وعبارة القنية : واختلف فيمن صلى وقد شمر كميه لعمل كان يعمله قبل الصلاة أو هيئته ذلك. وفيها أيضاً عن نجم الأئمة: وكان وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يكره السدل على القميص وعلى الإزار وقال : لأنه صنيع أهل الكتاب . فإن كان السدل بدون السراويل فكراهته لاحتمال كشف العورة عند الركوع ، وإن كان مع الأزار فكراهته لأجل التشبه بأهل الكتاب فهو مكروه مطلقاً، سواء كان للخيلاء أو لغيره للنهي من غير فصل اهـ. وفي فتح القدير أن السدل يصدق على أن يكون المنديل مرسلاً من كتفيه كما يعتاده كثير فينبغي لمن على عنقه منديل أن يضعه عند الصلاة، ويصدق أيضاً على لبس القباء من غير إدخال اليدين في كميه وقد صرح بالكراهة فيه .اهـ. وكذا صرح في النهاية بإدخال القباء المذكور في السدل وعزاه إلى مبسوط شيخ الإسلام والخلاصة لكن الذي في خلاصة الفتاوى : المصلي إذا كان لابساً شقة أو فرجيه ولم يدخل يديه اختلف المتأخرون في الكراهة والمختار أنه لا يكره اهـ. وظاهر ما في فتح القدير أن الشد الذي يعتاد وضعه على الكتفين إذا أرسل طرفاً على صدره وطرفاً على ظهره لا يخرج عن الكراهية فإنه عين الوضع، وظاهر كلامهم يقتضي أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون الثوب محفوظاً من الوقوع أو لا، فعلى هذا يكره في الطيلسان الذي يجعل على الرأس، وقد صرح به في شرح الوقاية وصرح العامة الحلبي بأن محل كراهة السدل عند عدم العذر، وأما عند العذر فلا كراهة وأنه إن كان للتكبر فهو مكروه مطلقاً . واختلف المشايخ في كراهة السدل خارج الصلاة كما في الدراية وصحح في القنية من باب الكراهية أنه لا يكره. ومن المكروه اشتمال الصماء لما رواه أبو داود عن ابن عمر قال قال رسول الله ( إذا كان لأحدكم ثوبان فليصل فيهما فإن لم يكن إلا ثوب فليتزر به ولا يشتمل اشتمال اليهود ) اهـ . واشتمال اليهود هو الصماء وهو إدارة الثوب على الجسد من غير إخراج اليد سمي بها لعدم منفذ يخرج يده منها كالصخرة الصماء، وفسرها في المحيط بأن يجمع طرفي ثوبه ويخرجهما تحت إحدى يديه على أحد كتفيه اهـ. وقيده في البدائع بأن لا يكون عليه سراويل . وإنما كره لأنه لا يؤمن انكشاف العورة، ومحمد رحمه الله فصل بين الاضطباغ ولبسة الصماء فقال : إنما تكره الصماء إذا لم يكن عليه إزار فإن كان عليه إزار فهو اضطباع لأن يدخل طرفي ثوبه تحت إحدى ضبعيه وهو مكروه لأنه لبس أهل الكبر اهـ. يرسل كميه في الصلاة ويقول لأن في إمساكهما كف الثوب وأنه مكروه ثم رمز إلى مجد الأئمة وغيره أنهم كانوا يمسكون ذلك. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وهو الأحوط .اهـ. قوله : (والمختار أنه لا يكره) قال الرملي : ومثله في البزازيه واختار قاضيخان وغيره أنه يكره وهو الصحيح. كذا ذكره الحلبي في شرح منية المصلي قوله: (وصحح في القنية أنه لا يكره قال في النهر أي تحريماً وإلا فمقتضى ما أنه يكره تنزيهاً .اهـ. وما مر هو قوله لأنه صنيع أهل الكتاب. قال الشيخ إسماعيل : وفيه بحث لأن الظاهر من كلامهم أن تخصيص أهل الكتاب بفعله معتبر فيه كونه في الصلاة فلا يظهر التشبه وكراهته خارجها فليتأمل.
Book of Prayer, Chapter on What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. And the Sadl (letting garment hang loosely), yawning, closing eyes, the imam standing (but not prostrating) in the arch, the imam being alone on a piece of cloth – all that is disliked (makruh). Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan and in al-Zahiriyya. And Ihtijar is disliked, which is wrapping the turban around the head and exposing the crown, as the ruffians do. In al-Muhit: Ihtijar is disliked because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it, and it is to roll up the turban and leave the middle of the head uncovered like the wicked. And it is said: it is to cover the nose with the turban like women's veils, either due to heat or cold or arrogance, and it is disliked due to the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'A man should not cover (his face) while praying.' In al-Mughrib: the interpretation of those who say it is wrapping the turban on the head and exposing the crown is more likely because it is derived from mi'jar of women, which is a cloth like a headband that a woman wraps around her head. Al-Mi'jar is on the pattern of minbar. Imam al-Walwaliji reasoned the dislike of Ihtijar as resembling the People of the Book. He said: it is disliked outside prayer, so inside it is more so. His statement: 'and folding his garment (kaff thawbayh)' due to the previous hadith, whether from in front of him or behind him when lowering for prostration. Kaff is to fold and gather. And because it abandons the sunna of the hands. It is mentioned in al-Mughrib from some that wearing the izaar over the qamis is from kaff. Thus, it is disliked to pray with the waist tied over the qamis and the like. It is explicitly stated in al-Atabiyya, reasoned as the practice of the People of the Book, but in al-Khulasa it is not disliked. Thus in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. Also included in kaff al-thawb is rolling up the sleeves, as in Fath al-Qadir, and its apparent meaning is general. In al-Khulasa and Munyat al-Musalli, the dislike is restricted to raising the sleeves to the elbows; apparently it is not disliked if raised less than that, but the general position is that it applies to all, because 'kaff al-thawb' applies to all. In al-Mujtaba there are two opinions on the dislike of rolling up the sleeves. In al-Quniyya, it is stated that holding the sleeves is more cautious (ahwat). In the Maliki school there is a detail: it is disliked if done for the prayer; if done for work and then one prays in that state, it is not disliked. And from kaff al-thawb is lifting it to avoid dust, as in Munyat al-Musalli, and it is said there is no harm in protecting it from dust, as in al-Mujtaba. From his statement: (and Sadl) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it, as recorded by Abu Dawud and al-Hakim and he declared it authentic. It is said: sadl al-thawb is to let it hang without folding its side. And it is said: it is to throw it over the head and let it down over the shoulders, and 'asdala' is a mistake. Thus in al-Mughrib. It is mentioned in al-Bada'i that al-Karkhi interpreted it as placing the garment on the head or shoulders and letting its ends hang from the sides if there is no sirwal. Tashmir: His statement: (and the general position is debatable) if its authority is what he mentioned from Fath al-Qadir, because al-Kamal ibn al-Humam, although he gave a general statement here, later restricted his words when discussing branches, saying: 'Prayer with the sleeve above the forearm is also disliked.' So there is no contradiction between him and al-Khulasa and Munyat al-Musalli. Thus in al-Sharnabaliyya. His statement: (and in the Maliki school there is a detail etc.) He said in al-Nahr: mentioned in al-Quniyya that if one rolled up his sleeves for work he was doing before prayer, there is disagreement on the dislike – and it is apparent that it is disliked if done for the prayer itself. The expression of al-Quniyya: 'And they differed about one who prays while having rolled up his sleeves for work he was doing before prayer, or that was his habit.' And in it also from Najm al-A'imma: And from Abu Hanifa that he disliked sadl over the qamis and over the izaar, and he said: because it is the practice of the People of the Book. If the sadl is without a sirwal, then its dislike is due to the possibility of the 'awra being exposed during ruku'; if with an izaar, then its dislike is due to resembling the People of the Book. So it is disliked absolutely, whether out of arrogance or otherwise, due to the prohibition without distinction. In Fath al-Qadir: Sadl applies to a handkerchief hanging from the shoulders as many do, so one who has a handkerchief around his neck should remove it when praying. It also applies to wearing a cloak (qaba') without inserting the hands into its sleeves, and he explicitly stated its dislike. Similarly, in al-Nihaya, the mentioned cloak is included in sadl, and he attributed it to the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam and al-Khulasa. But in Khulasat al-Fatawa: 'The one praying, if he is wearing a shiqqa or farjiyya and did not insert his hands, the later scholars differed on the dislike, and the chosen view is that it is not disliked.' The apparent meaning of what is in Fath al-Qadir is that the shadd (a scarf) habitually placed on the shoulders, if one end is let down on the chest and the other on the back, does not escape dislike, for it is exactly that position. The apparent meaning of their words is that there is no difference whether the garment is prevented from falling or not. Thus, it is disliked in the taylasan placed on the head, as explicitly stated in Sharh al-Wiqaya. And al-Halabi explicitly stated that the place of dislike of sadl is when there is no excuse, and if there is an excuse, there is no dislike, and if it is for arrogance, it is disliked absolutely. The mashayikh differed on the dislike of sadl outside prayer, as in al-Diraya, and in al-Quniyya it is declared authentic that it is not disliked. Among the disliked actions is Ishtimal al-Samma'. For Abu Dawud narrated from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'If one of you has two garments, let him pray in them; if he has only one garment, let him wrap it around his waist (yattazir) and not wrap it in the manner of the Jews (ishtimal al-yahud).' And the wrapping of the Jews is al-Samma'. It is the wrapping of the garment around the body without taking out the hands. It is called samma' because there is no outlet for the hands, like a solid rock (sakhra samma'). In al-Muhit, it is interpreted as gathering the two ends of the garment and bringing them under one of his arms onto one shoulder. In al-Bada'i, it is restricted to when there is no sirwal. It is disliked because exposure of the 'awra is not safe from it. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) distinguished between idtiba' and the dress of samma'. He said: 'Samma' is only disliked if there is no izaar; if there is an izaar, then it is idtiba' (wrapping with one shoulder bare) – and that is disliked because it is the dress of the arrogant.' He lets his sleeves hang in prayer and says: because holding them is kaff al-thawb and it is disliked. Then he gestured to Majd al-A'imma and others that they used to hold them. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'That is more cautious (ahwat).' His statement: (and the chosen view is that it is not disliked) al-Ramli said: 'And similar is in al-Bazzaziyya, and Qadi Khan and others chose that it is disliked, and it is the correct view.' Thus mentioned by al-Halabi in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. His statement: (and in al-Quniyya it is declared authentic that it is not disliked) He said in al-Nahr: 'i.e., as tahrimi? Otherwise, the apparent meaning is that it is makruh tanzihi.' And what passed is his statement: 'because it is the practice of the People of the Book.' Shaykh Ismail said: 'There is discussion in this, for the apparent meaning of their words is that the specification of the People of the Book by this action is considered when it is in prayer; so resemblance and its dislike outside prayer are not apparent. So let it be considered.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked (makruh) in it. The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on several disliked actions, providing definitions, proofs from hadith, and the reasoning (ta'lil) for their rulings. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from primary sources and later scholarly consensus.

First, the text lists general disliked actions: sadl (letting the garment hang loosely), yawning, closing the eyes, the imam standing in an arch without prostrating, and the imam praying alone on a piece of cloth. All are ruled makruh based on prior authoritative works like Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Zahiriyya.

Then focuses on al-Ihtijar (اعتجار): wrapping the turban around the head while leaving the crown exposed, as done by ruffians. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it. It is disliked because it resembles wicked people or, according to some interpretations, involves covering the nose like women's veils. Imam al-Walwaliji adds that it is a resemblance to the People of the Book, so it is disliked even outside prayer, and more so inside it.

Next, al-Kaff (كف الثوب): folding or gathering the garment, such as folding the garment in front or behind during prostration. This abandons the sunna of letting the hands hang. Wearing the izaar (waist wrapper) above the qamis (shirt) is considered kaff. Some Hanafi sources differ: 'al-Atabiyya' rules it makruh as resembling the People of the Book, while 'al-Khulasa' says it is not disliked. Ibn Nujaym notes a restriction: raising sleeves to the elbows is makruh, but less than that may be permissible. There are two opinions in 'al-Mujtaba'. The cautious view (ahwat) is to hold the sleeves. The passage also discusses sadl in detail: its definitions, the narration from Abu Dawud and al-Hakim (authenticated), and the opinion of Abu Hanifa that it is makruh over both the qamis and izaar because it is the practice of the People of the Book. If no sirwal (trousers) is worn, the added risk of 'awra exposure during ruku' increases the dislike. Sadl applies to letting a handkerchief or cloak hang loosely. On the issue of sadl outside prayer, there is disagreement; the authenticated view in 'al-Quniyya' is that it is not disliked, but it is still makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful) as some interpret.

Finally, Ishtimal al-Samma' (اشتمال الصماء): wrapping the entire body in a garment without any opening for the hands, similar to the Jewish practice. The hadith from Ibn Umar prohibits it. It is called samma' because it resembles a solid rock (sakhra samma') with no outlet. It is makruh because it may expose the 'awra. Imam Muhammad distinguishes: if there is an izaar underneath, it becomes idtiba' (wrapping with one shoulder bare), which is also disliked as the dress of the arrogant. The passage ends by noting that holding the sleeves during prayer is considered kaff and is disliked, but many early scholars actually held them, so the author says that holding them is more cautious (ahwat).

Key Terms
السدل (al-sadl): Letting the garment hang loosely without folding its side, or throwing it over the head and shoulders. Also includes wearing a cloak without inserting hands into sleeves.
الاعتجار (al-ihtijar): Wrapping the turban around the head while leaving the crown exposed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on ihtijar (wrapping the turban leaving the crown exposed) during prayer?
A) It is permissible (mubah) because the crown is not 'awra.
B) It is disliked (makruh) because it resembles the wicked and the People of the Book.
C) It is forbidden (haram) because it explicitly violates a Quranic command.
D) It is recommended (sunnah) because it keeps the head cool.

Key Takeaway
Ihtijar and sadl are makruh in prayer due to resemblance to the People of the Book and risk of 'awra exposure; outside prayer, sadl is not disliked according to the chosen view.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Kaff al-Thawb and Ishtimal al-Samma' in Prayer
Commentary on disliked actions in prayer with definitions, proofs, and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kaff al-thawb and ishtimal al-samma', state their rulings, and apply the Hanafi distinction between samma' and idtiba'.

Core Idea
Folding the garment (kaff) and wrapping without arm outlet (ishtimal al-samma') are makruh because they contradict the sunna and resemble non-Muslims.

This lesson examines two more disliked actions: kaff al-thawb (folding or gathering the garment, including rolling up sleeves) and ishtimal al-samma' (wrapping the entire body in a garment with no opening for the hands). The Prophet specifically prohibited the latter, and both are considered makruh for resembling the Jews or the arrogant. The Hanafi school also distinguishes between samma' (without izaar) and idtiba' (with izaar), both disliked.

Guided Path
1
Define the acts
Kaff al-thawb is folding or gathering the garment, such as rolling up sleeves or wearing the izaar over the qamis. Ishtimal al-samma' is wrapping the garment around the body without any opening for the hands, resembling a solid rock.
Ishtimal al-samma' is also called the wrapping of the Jews.
2
Identify the proofs
The proof for kaff al-thawb is that it abandons the sunna of letting the hands hang, and it is the practice of the People of the Book. The proof for ishtimal al-samma' is the hadith from Ibn Umar: 'Do not wrap in the manner of the Jews.'
The hadith is cited in the passage.
3
Understand the ta'lil
Kaff is disliked because it resembles the People of the Book and abandons the sunna. Ishtimal al-samma' is disliked because it may expose the 'awra and is explicitly prohibited.
Imam Muhammad distinguishes: without izaar, it is samma' (disliked); with izaar, it is idtiba' (also disliked as dress of the arrogant).
4
Note intra-Hanafi differences
On kaff: some sources say wearing izaar over qamis is not disliked (al-Khulasa), while others say it is (al-Atabiyya). On rolling sleeves: there are two opinions. The cautious view (ahwat) is to hold the sleeves.
On ishtimal: the standard view is that it is makruh; Muhammad's distinction is additional.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Kaff al-thawb is generally makruh, but the specific ruling on izaar over qamis is that it is not disliked according to the chosen view. Ishtimal al-samma' is makruh without izaar; with izaar, it becomes idtiba' which is also makruh.
The tarjih authority is Ibn Nujaym and later Hanafi works.
